==PAGE:3==
Abstract 
 
 
While the form of building construction delivery known as Integrated Design-Build (or Integrated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Project Delivery) is necessary for handling the complexity of modern projects, it raises up a host of
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
problems due to the amount and variety of stakeholders that are brought together to co-design.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Namely, the difficulty in forming a shared mental model of expectations regarding the project can
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cause disappointment in the results, as well as time and cost overruns. This thesis is about creating
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
an intervention to alleviate those issues. Employing knowledge from the field of mental models as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
well as the philosophy of individualism and the use of rhetoric in design, two Integrated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design-Build workshops were analyzed to extract a set of rhetorical elements common to all such
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sessions. Then using Per Halstrøm’s framework of rhetorical design as basis, and drawing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inspiration from the aforementioned body of knowledge, a design game was formulated around the
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
empirical data by an iterative design process, following established design game theory. The game
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was found to indeed more than double the alignment of a group’s individual mental models, though
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
more testing is needed to verify this. A roadmap was also provided for bolstering the game into a
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
universally applicable intervention.

==PAGE:6==
​1​ Introduction 
 
 
Below I describe the practical and academic problems whence the need for this research sources.
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Then I outline the theoretical direction of the intervention that I shall formulate in order to tackle
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
those problems. Lastly I lay out the aim and objectives of the intervention and delineate the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
research methodology to achieve it. 
 
​1.1​ Gaps, needs and issues 
 
​1.1.1​ Practical 
 
Nowadays the result of architects’ and design managers’ toil is not simply buildings, but complex
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
sociotechnical systems, endowed with high performance and ‘smartness’. And given the increased
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
strictness of regulations and the raising of owner demands, it is no wonder that in the past few
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decades the early stages of sociotechnical system design have come to accommodate a substantial
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
array of actors to complement the architects’ capabilities. (Urup, 2016) 
This modern form of project delivery goes by the name Integrated Design-Build (Urup, 2016)
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
or Integrated Project Delivery. While this shift has been critical to achieving the ability to fulfil
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
present-day building requirements, it has brought about a surge in complexity due not only to
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
increasing the amount of diverse knowledge to be fused together, but also to the large and dynamic
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
amount of stakeholders, which summons forth both the benefits and the dark side of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
heterogeneous collaboration; considering that there is bound to be some level of friction even in
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
collaboration amongst professionals of similar backgrounds, it is obvious that teams endowed with
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
diverse (and occasionally divergent) norms, interests and values are fertile ground for inefficiency
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and discord, sometimes even strife. Ugly manifestations of the curse of human nature tend to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
foment in egocentric groups when a common ground is not readily available: insufficient
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
knowledge sharing (Thuesen, 2006), lack of coordination (Apelgren, et al., 2005), even collective
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
shirking from responsibilities (Henisz et al., 2012). Every designer is familiar with the emotional
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
effects of this: “​frustration, alienation, insecurity, hopelessness and unrest​” (Gomes & Tzortzopoulos). And
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
the results are deplorable: significant time overruns, excessive construction costs, and low quality
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the final sociotechnical system (Apelgren et al., 2005; Schultz, 2012). 
5 
==PAGE:7==
Integrated Design-Build projects tend to include in their early stage a Team Alignment
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
Workshop, which “​has two primary functions: it creates clarity, consensus, and commitment to project goals,
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
and it begins to shape a project culture that transforms a group of individuals into a high-performance team​ ”
   
   
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
   
 
 
A facilitator uses his / her preferred techniques to achieve this; a common procedure is to make the
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
     
 
 
participants create a list of values for the project (Allison et al., 2018). However, considering the
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
negative phenomena mentioned above, it is clear that this kind of methodology is often insufficient. 
 
 
​1.1.2​ Theoretical 
 
How is this problem understood in mainstream academia, and what kinds of directions have been
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
suggested for solving it? 
There are two steps to grasping the origins of the troublesome nature of such collaboration.
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
Firstly, there is the design problem's ‘wickedness’: it is difficult to define and inherently unsolvable
 
   
 
 
 
     
   
 
 
 
 
(i.e. no right or wrong answers, only good or bad). Rittel and Weber (1973) defined ‘wicked problem’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
by ten characteristics; one that is particularly pertinent to architecture/construction, due to the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
immobile nature of the results, is that the solution is a ‘one-shot’ operation, without possibility for
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
trial and error. However, according to Coyne (2005), all problems are to some extent wicked, even
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
simple puzzles, for though there be a definite solution and a definite set of rules, the problem is
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
nevertheless experienced in a complex mental context of motivations, skills and tendencies. Puzzles
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
simply exhibit diminished wickedness due to their rules and constraints. As such, it is no wonder
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
that ambiguous, open-ended design processes such as Integrated Design-Build are notorious for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their alarming levels of wickedness (Bresnen & Marshall, 2010) (which would be easy to quantify by
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
monitoring designers’ stress hormone levels).  
Secondly, the vexing characteristics associated with ‘wickedness’ hamper the formation of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
shared understanding within design teams. Not only is it often difficult to communicate the details
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
   
 
 
 
of the problem and solution domains across different disciplinary ‘languages’ (Bucciarelli, 2002), but
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it is also common to find a lack of robust understanding about how other members of the team
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
operate, and about the dependencies between members’ tasks. Moreover, particularly in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
construction projects, the concept of ‘collaboration’ itself is a major missing piece in shared
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
understanding, hindering integrative practices (Zimina et al., 2012). As such, members' ability to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discern incompatible design decisions is often poor, engendering excessive iteration (Urup, 2016).  
Although the origins of the issue are hence relatively soundly understood, the process of its
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
development, or on the contrary the development of smooth and effective design operation, is not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
so well charted out. It is beneficial to think of shared understanding in terms of the formation of a
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
     
shared mental model. Several methodologies based on mental models have been used to study the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
above problems. For example, Badke-Schaub and Casakin’s method for quantifying mental model
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6 
==PAGE:8==
sharedness in design using an utterance categorization technique seems a valuable tool for gaining
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
a simple overview of a team’s mental models, and insights into how team composition affects them
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Badke-Schaub & Casakin, 2017). Another notable study is by Cash et al. (2007), who used concept
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mapping to examine the effectiveness of question-asking as a catalyst for mental model alignment.
Despite the interesting insights presented in these inquiries, they are mostly descriptive,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focused on quantifying the overall dynamics of collaboration. But the purpose of this field is not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
simply to comprehend design, but to exploit that comprehension in order to take action, to wage
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
war against that which defiles design, yielding a purer, more effective practice with which to enrich
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
the world. As the majority of the existing literature on this topic does not offer concepts of robust
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
tools for rectifying what they highlighted as lacking, I intend to conceptualize such a tool.  
 
 
​1.2​ Glimmer of hope 
 
 
​1.2.1​ Maligned methodology 
 
One of the drawbacks that I can discern in the inquiries described above is that they do not directly
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
address the role of rhetoric, which seems to me an auspicious viewpoint on the issue. In fact,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
despite originating from Aristotle’s mind, rhetoric has in general been ignored throughout the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
millennia. Although it surfaced again in the 1950, this powerful field is even now underutilized in
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
academia, and even more so in design practice. 
Halstrøm argues for the significance of persuasion in the design process, contending for an
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
explicitly rhetorical view of it. Even though existing models of the process of design may be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
accurate, “​they provide little advice on how to deliberate and make judgments in terms of intention and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
argumentation for designers.​” (Halstrøm, 2017) This argumentation should be present both in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution, which should induce the user to espouse certain values and ways of acting, and in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design process itself, so that the designers can understand the reasoning behind the decisions they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
make. Using classical rhetorical concepts, Halstrøm crafted a holistic model to help designers
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
clarify their intentions and persuasively argue their way through design tasks.  
The simplicity and examples of successful application of this framework have persuaded me
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
of its effectiveness in enlightening practitioners and raising the lasting quality of their work. And
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as the aligning of mental models is intuitively very much a stream of argumentation between team
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
members, a rhetorical orientation towards the issue of shared understanding seems rife with
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
potential.  
7 
==PAGE:9==
 
 
​1.2.2​ Parasitic solution 
 
One of the benefits of Halstrøm’s model is that it shifts the focus of designing away from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution space into a dynamic deliberation on the whole design space: the solution, problem and
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
audience spaces (Halstrøm, 2017). As such, rather than aiding the finding of solutions to a problem,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
     
 
it promotes the finding of solutions to multiple problems, alongside the audiences i.e. user
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
segments that each affects. It is therefore fitting to now announce that the problem space of this
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project, too, auspiciously expanded during its Analysis phase (Chapter 3). Namely, I discovered that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
not only is the rhetorical model well suited for tackling the issue of poor sharing of understanding,
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
but it also has high potential for raising the quality and quantity of that understanding.  
To illustrate, let us look at two examples of impressive projects that upon scrutiny reveal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
remarkably simple design oversights. Copenhagen’s Inner Harbor Bridge (Figure 1), opened in 2016,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
is a massive success when considering its primary function of enabling light traffic across the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
canal: the city estimated a daily use of around 7000 cyclists, but the current figure is about 16,000.
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
Yet it abounds with design blunders, even when ignoring the subjective issue of such an epitome of
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
contemporary sleekness being in total juxtaposition to the delicate classicism that pervades the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
surrounding urban fabric. Its designers in Studio Bednarski, which is based in two countries that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
are hardly at the forefront of urban cycling culture, have failed to consider established cycling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
standards. (Colville-Andersen, 2017) 
Firstly, the bridge’s core concept is the idea of its two halves ‘kissing’ in the middle
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(manifesting most spectacularly in its glass panels changing color as it opens). For some reason, this
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
gimmick was hard to reconcile with common sense, which hence had to yield. Cyclists have to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
maneuver around two sharp turns, a design decision that asserts the righteousness of ignoring
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users’ comfort and safety (established for over a century in infrastructural Best Practice standards)
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
in favor of visual quirkiness. An even graver oversight is that due to an inability to surmise that a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
bridge designer could not be satisfied with straight lanes, unsuspecting cyclists tend to hit the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
break too late when approaching the ‘kiss’ at high speed; one can sometimes find skidmarks that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stop a bike-length away perpendicularly from the central colored panel, without which many a
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
commuter would have ended up altogether refreshed. The city has thus slapped a warning sign on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
the panels. Other problems include the bridge’s slippery surface during winter and its initially
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
man-sized holes in the railings making it superb for drunk people to commute by, as well as its
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ominous skewing and bending in Spring when the air suddenly warms but the water remains cold.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
After overruns in time and money of 3 years and 100 million DKK respectively, this is the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
outstanding result that the citizens got. (Colville-Andersen, 2017) 
 
8 
==PAGE:10==
 
Figure 1: Copenhagen’s Inner Harbor Bridge. Right: warning signs set up by the city after finding out unsuspecting
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cyclists’ tendency to bump into the colored panel. The signs can be thought of as works of abstract art conveying the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
beauty of a designer’s mind as he/she nobly resists the suffocating forces of common sense. 
 
Berlin’s Holocaust Memorial (Figure 2) is composed of 2711 concrete blocks arranged in an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
orthogonal grid, varying in height but not length or width. At the site’s perimeter, the blocks are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
less than eight inches tall, and increase in height towards the center, where they are sixteen feet
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
high. The site’s appearance of a disorienting, halted construction project contrasts with the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
surrounding city’s vivacity; its designer, architect Peter Eisenman, succeeded in conveying a feeling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
of dreadful monotony, of a sinister rationality. However, up close it affects us rather differently. At
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
its periphery, the diverse spaces formed by the low-slung blocks are a gentle invitation to step in.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
Venturing towards the center, where the monoliths rise while the ground slopes downwards, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designer meant for us to feel an eerie foreboding, a sense of descending into an unknown place
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
while being gradually trapped, perhaps like approaching Hell through its suburbs. However, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actual experience is more like a Haunted Mansion thrill. The rectilinear grid structure causes any
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fledgling fear to dispel, as it warrants lines of sight back to the bustling streets at constant and
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
short intervals. And we know that we cannot become truly lost when we are presented with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
straight escape corridor behind every corner; the axes even invite us to explore further the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
pleasantly mysterious place. (Goldhagen, 2017) 
In addition, the popularity of the Memorial as a tourist destination fills the peripheries with
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
people (eg. children jumping between blocks), driving away the sense of loneliness that is salient in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
inducing horror. “​Eisenman’s intentions do not translate into the built reality because his use of the grid as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
the datum by which to arrange the concrete slabs undermines the very experience it is meant to effectuate​.​”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
   
 
(Goldhagen, 2017) The Memorial fails to embody his stated agenda for two reasons. Firstly, he
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
neglected all the stimuli that visitors would process; though they proprioceptively sense themselves
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
descending, their visual sense enables them to maintain their sense of location. Secondly, he
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designed the Memorial as if the visitor experiences it in isolation, failing to recognize that places
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are composed not only of physical but also social structures, the life they facilitate. (Goldhagen,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2017) 
9 
==PAGE:11==
 
 
Figure 2: Berlin’s Holocaust Memorial. 
 
The shortcomings in these projects could easily have been prevented if the designers had taken the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
effort to deliberate not only on the aspects of the design space that ​
thought were most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
important, but on a wide variety of common architectural topics that may not at first seem
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relevant, such as visual connection, noise, clarity of navigation, dynamics of cycling, and time of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
year. Now, as Halstrøm’s model is all about approaching design from a multitude of angles, i.e.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
topics (as elucidated in Section 2.3.4), there is no reason why I should refrain from incorporating
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
this inspirational power into my eventual intervention. It should however be kept in mind that as
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this project is directed towards the initial stage of Integrated Design-Build, where concepts should
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not yet be concretized, it is sufficient to simply cover the topics without refining any ensuing ideas.  
 
 
 
 
10 
==PAGE:12==
​1.3​ Research design 
 
I will here lay out this project’s overall aim, followed by the concrete steps that I will take in order
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
to reach them. They are directly derived from the issues and the promising glimpses of solution
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented above.  
 
 
​1.3.1​ Targets and questions 
 
Aim 
To create an intervention for early-stage Integrated Design-Build meetings that would alleviate the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
problems originating from inadequate formation of a shared mental model of expectations. 
 
Outcomes 
The intervention shall have the following outcomes: 
1.
Aligns the participants’ mental models of what the result of their project should and what it
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
could be like. 
2.
Enables the group to consider a wide array of the most important aspects of the design
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
space (problems, solutions, and users) early on to diminish the need for creative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
workarounds later. 
 
Research questions 
1.
What is the theoretical basis of the problem? In other words, what are theoretically the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
bottlenecks that inhibit smooth and fruitful Integrated Design-Build co-design? 
2.
How can the rhetorical elements that are important in Integrated Design-Build co-design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be found out? 
3.
What kind of intervention would be most efficient in achieving the expected outcomes? 
4.
How can such an intervention be strengthened from a proof of concept into a powerful tool
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
   
 
 
that would be beneficial in any Integrated Design-Build project? 
 
 
 
11 
==PAGE:13==
​1.3.2​ Research methodology 
 
Here I describe the methodology that I will use to answer each of the research questions. 
1.
I will gather together a literature review that explains the problem space outlined above,
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
delineated into two sections. The review will begin with the philosophical roots of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
individualism and collectivism as well as its psychological manifestations, which my
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intuition identifies as the heart of the issues mentioned. Next I will expound the theory and
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
practice of mental models, as they seem to be the bridge between the psychological
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
foundations of the problem space and the phenomena observed in design practice. 
2.
After laying out the basis for understanding the rhetorical subspace of the solution space in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
the third section of the literature review, I shall then go out into the field, collecting data
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from Integrated Design-Build co-design sessions. Thereafter I will analyze the data by
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
transcribing it, and extracting from it rhetorical information. Thence I can code the data
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
with the rhetorical information in order to solidify it with statistical qualities.  
3.
The rhetorical section of the literature review having already kickstarted vague probing of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the solution (and audience) space, I will amplify it by explaining in another section of the
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
literature review the use of metaphors in rhetorics. Furthermore, in the last section of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
literature review, I will give an overview of design game theory so as to gain concrete
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elements for developing the solution, which will be underpinned by the findings from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
empirical analysis. I shall approach the development process by informal application of
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Halstrøm’s model, laying out the values of the project and amplifying them. For the actual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design process, I shall follow the classical ​plan-act-observe-reflect formula (Robson, 1993),
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
though the proof-of-concept nature of this project warrants an informal approach, where I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
integrate ​reflection + planning​, and ​acting + observing​, into alternate steps. In other words, for
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
each version of the intervention, I first explain my design decisions and their physical
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
outcome, then describe how I tested the version and the learnings that I gained. 
4.
I shall try to find the weaknesses in my analytic process, regarding both the collection of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
data and its interpretation. Thus I can formulate courses of action to overhaul the analytic
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
process in order to be able to develop a far sounder empirical basis that would morph the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intervention from ‘proof of concept’ into ‘powerful, universally applicable concept’. 
 
 
 
12 
==PAGE:14==
​2​ Literature review 
 
This chapter answers my first research question, “​What is the theoretical basis of the problem? In other
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
words, what are theoretically the bottlenecks that inhibit smooth and fruitful Integrated Design-Build
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
co-design?​” 
 
I will begin this quest by exposing the problem’s philosophical kernel, rooting subsequent analysis
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and synthesis in sound and potent conceptual ground. Next I contextualize the problem in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
modern comprehension of shared understanding in terms of mental models, outlining cutting-edge
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
practical inquiries. Then follows a review of the art of rhetoric, and how design can be expressed as
 
 
 
   
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
its application to the realm of the artificial. Therein I describe Halstrøm’s practical framework. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
following section is a reflection on one of the most critical rhetorical elements, metaphors, with
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
examples of e.g. those used by hospital designers and healthcare practitioners. This chapter
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concludes with a review of basic design game theory and a brief analysis of two design games. 
 
 
​2.1​ Individualism and collectivism 
 
Everything in an ecosystem affects everything else. As John Muir, an ecologist thanks to whom
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Californian redwood forests have been preserved, put it:  
“​When we try to pick out anything by itself, we find it hitched to everything else in the Universe.​”
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
   
 
 
(Muir, 1911) 
However, does this universal interconnectedness cover the domain of human thought and behavior
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
too? Does it permeate the ‘unnatural’ realm of society? Let us find out. 
​2.1.1​ Culture 
 
Hofstede’s influential work on contrasting country-level societies based on individualism gave rise
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to an ample strain of cross-cultural research. The importance of Hofstede’s formulation can be said
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
to lie in his organizing cultural differences into overarching patterns, such as ‘power distance’ and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘masculinity’, facilitating rich analysis of their causes and their effects on economies and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
13 
==PAGE:15==
professional activities. Particularly when contrasting Western and East Asian cultural frames,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
where the effects tend to be the most blatant, individualism is seen as the opposite of collectivism.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
Protestantism and Enlightenment-induced democratization are seen as the roots of the Western
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
focus on self-actualization and personal freedom (Oyserman et al., 2002). The influence of ancient
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
philosophical and spiritual traditions, as well as the communal coordination required for rice
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
farming (Talheim et al.), have had the opposite effect in East Asia, shaping a culture of social
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
harmony and concealment of personal feelings and desires. 
Various conceptualizations of individualism have been suggested. Hofstede defined
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
individualism as a “​focus on rights above duties, a concern for oneself and immediate family, an emphasis on
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
personal autonomy and self-fulfillment, and the basing of one’s identity on one’s personal accomplishments.​”
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Hofstede, 1980) Schwartz (1990) defined individualistic societies as built upon contracts, with social
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relations suffused by obligations and expectations aimed at preserving and achieving status. All of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
the definitions tend to frame individualism as egocentric and society-peripheralizing. (Oyserman et
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
al., 2002) 
The Japanese post-war economic success is to a large extent attributable to business
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
practices and management techniques molded to suit the local collectivist mindset. Attention on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this trend has coincided with the increased focus on team-based work in Western organizations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(McGrath, 1997; Ilgen, 1999), giving rise to a vast array of methodologies for boosting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cooperative capabilities of groups. In addition, many management scholars have championed for a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
fundamental transformation of Western work culture towards more collectivist systems (Locke et
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
al., 2001).   
But the reality of these cultural differences is not as trivial as the above may suggest. It
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
would be foolish to outright vilify individualism, for it is not wholly lacking in virtue: it has been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
shown to kindle creativity more effectively than a collectivist approach to group work. Such an
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
approach is, however, beneficial only when creativity is a supreme goal, which is usually not the
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
case. (Goncalo & Staw, 2006). 
We clearly need to understand the philosophical complexities of individualism in order to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
take action in an optimal way. So as to gain an overview of its basic characteristics and an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding of its historical emergence, as well as to lay out a map of collectivism in light of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
which to craft possible interventions, I now proceed onto a survey of the quest for Self. 
 
 
 
14 
==PAGE:16==
​2.1.2​ What is the foundation of Self?  ‘​I think, hence I am​’ 
 
Descartes’ legacy permeates not only Western philosophy, but to some extent general thinking too.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
The most influential notions sourcing from René's prolific mind are those relating to Being, in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
particular to the nature of individual experience. In his view, not only is the subject separate from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
the world of objects, but the ​individual subject is supreme over everything else; each person exists
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alone, an independent island of thought. Descartes’ ​Discourse on Method​, in which he lays out these
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ideas, is one man's utterly solitary journey of philosophical discovery; his most famous phrase
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
begins with ‘​I am​’ rather than ‘​Man is​’. As the reader is likely to have lived most of his/her life in a
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
Cartesian-suffused culture (i.e. more or less all of the industrialized world), such a view may seem
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
wholly obvious, rousing slight bemusement at the need to state it formally. However, the primacy
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
of the self is not a fundamental, biologically determined ingredient of culture: Illich and Saunders
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(1988) assert that while there is a first-person singular pronoun in every language, most lack words
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
expressing ‘self’. These languages tend to be orally transmitted, implying that the invention of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
permanent records and the emergence of individual authors, beginning in Mesopotamia and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
spreading into much of Eurasia, gave birth to the ‘self’. Descartes then simply formalized it into the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
canon of Western intellectualism. (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1993) 
Descartes’ individualism rests also on a capacity for independent reasoning. According to
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
him, it is possible to shut out all influencers of the mind: tradition, dogma, social pressure etc. so as
     
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to reach truth; ​Transparency of Mind is his term for such a sterilized cognitive state. The path by
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
which any truth, according to the philosopher, can be reached in such a state, is the general method
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
of inference that he adapted from geometric inference. Having filtered through the brains of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
empiricists, chiefly Locke and Hume, the belief in ​Transparency of Mind burgeoned into the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reverence for ‘objectivity’ that pervades modern science. (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1993) 
This belief in independence of reason causes self-delusion, for it induces us to accept as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
self-evident most or all of the views we hold at any moment. Heidegger noted that we are prone to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
setting aspects of our beliefs beyond the range of doubt. In effect, by sheltering within the walls of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
‘clarity of vision’, the modern mind more or less subconsciously seeks to avert the effort required
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for, and pain occasioning from, any reconfiguration of beliefs. And when such adjustment turns out
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
unavoidable, the modern mind frames the process as a growth in clarity of vision; the independent
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
mind is on its independent, self-sufficient quest for truth. (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1993) 
It is then assumed that the collective rises from mere agglomeration of the instances of the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
individual. That a group’s behaviors, opinions and emotional states are derived from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interactions between those of its constituents. In this view there is no flow in the other direction:
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
reasoning, an individual act, is divorced from all context, all social dynamic. A modern framing of
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
15 
==PAGE:17==
this idea, prevalent in cognitive science, is the characterization of the mind as a mere processor of
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
information (Newell and Simon, 1972). 
 
​2.1.3​ Heroism ensues 
 
If you have been wondering, “​Can this kind of cold philosophy really have had such a profound effect on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
Western culture?​”, you have good intuition. For René fathered by his work not only the taciturn child
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Rationalism, which these ideas of the subject-object divide and clarity of mind comprise, but pretty
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
and raucous Romanticism too. What the rationalist philosophers omitted, romantic ones such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Hegel, Kant and Fichte appropriated, exploring that which is ​subjective​: emotion, aesthetics, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
human spirit, mystery, and so on. More practical philosophers, commonly called ​artists​, fashioned
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
out the Romantic subculture to balance the scientific ethos, bestowing humanity with arguably its
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
most celebrated period of art (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1993). Romanticism is the exploration of an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
individual experience, and the artist is the noblest individual. In Schlegel’s words, “​What man is to
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
the other creatures on earth, the artist is to man​”. 
Herein lies a critical kernel of the problem that I (or perhaps rather ​we​) seek to tackle. Like
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
fine art, design is held to be a mysterious process springing from the depths of an individual's
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
creativity. The designer’s mind is a ‘fountainhead’ (Rand, 1972) that deserves to flow freely, and
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
obnoxious are the forces of approval and consensus that strive to curb its sublimity. Democratic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
government, the ubiquity of collaborative work, and the pervasiveness of knowledge about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
manifestations of vile 20th-century ‘isms’, have biased the modern human to rather trivially
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
attribute problems to collectivism, invoking ‘mob rule’, groupthink, following the crowd, etc.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
against which individuals should wage war (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1993). And having resisted the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enemy to the last, the designer acquires the aura of a hero: “​The great creators—the thinkers, the artists,
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the scientists, the inventors—stood alone against the men of their time. Every great new thought was opposed ...
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
But the men of unborrowed vision went ahead. They fought, they suffered and they paid. But they won.​”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Rand, 1972, p. 664)   
This is not to say that Romanticism is ‘wrong’ (I daresay most readers of this work relish
   
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some of its fruits, to say the least) or that the special nature of artists in society is a foolish idea, or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
     
 
 
 
that there is no need for the elusive ‘creative spark’ (ie. abduction) in design; the point is simply
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
that this ideology, as ideologies tend to do, has seeped from its core domain into a place where it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
does more harm than good. Namely, into collaborative work, particularly in design. 
 
 
16 
==PAGE:18==
​2.1.4​ So lets try again.  ‘​I am in the world​’ 
 
Heidegger dissolves the primordiality of the subject-object view, asserting that the dualistic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Cartesian metaphor is insufficient to act as an ontological foundation for the concept of Being. In
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
place of the lofty independent self, Heidegger posits ​Dasein​, or ​Being-in-the-world​. These terms do
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not mean simply spatial inclusion. Rather, they abound with connotations of rich being-in:
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dwelling, communing, being intimate with. Heidegger criticizes the Cartesian tendency to consider
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
‘knowing’ as the defining quality of the subject and its primary way of interacting with objects, for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
the question remains of how the subject transcends its boundaries to interact with the world.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Heidegger hence suggests ‘knowing’ to be only one mode of Being-in-the-world. Instead of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
self-consciousness, the mode of being, or level of experience, is what defines the Dasein’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
self-understanding; the foundation of the self is not pure consciousness, but an understanding
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
determined by mood. (Çüçen, 1998) 
Below is a summary of Heidegger's four-level ontology (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1993): 
1.
The primary experience of the world is undifferentiated. The self is wholly absorbed in the
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
world: “​we often experience ourselves as active yet are not aware of what we are trying to do​”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Dreyfus, 1991, p. 94). So much so that the completion of a task comes as a surprise.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
Heidegger’s well-known example of this mode is of using a hammer: the most primordial
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
experience of using a hammer is its ​handiness (​Zuhandheit​). This quality of availability is not
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
knowledge, but a pure experience of using it, of its being part of us and of the world . 
1
2.
Next is the level of the ​meaningful​, the ​unavailable (​unzuhanden​), which comes about when the
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘readiness to hand’ ceases. When encountering the unexpected, or failing to cope with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
situation, or plunging into reflection, entities are experienced as differentiated from the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
self and the world; they take on shades of meaning. For example, if a hammer breaks when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
using it, one becomes aware of its value (and if one hits his/her thumb instead of the nail,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
value is ascribed to the thumb too). This is the mode at work in daily conversation and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
casual thoughts. 
3.
The third level of experience is that of ​theoretical reflection, of purposeful detachment from
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
the object. This is the realm of science, where one analyzes the world and interacts with
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
theories, where objects are measurable entities. 
4.
The final level is that of pure contemplation, an engagement with bare facts, devoid of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
feelings. It is the rarest and most fragile encounter with the world, with any sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
purpose or prejudice shattering it. Here the subject is self-sufficient and disinterested.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Indeed, this mode is equivalent to Descartes’ ontology. 
1 Aristotle too argued for the primordiality of ready-to-hand for existence. Heidegger implied that though Aristotle’s direction was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
right, his formulation missed the deep structure of ready-to-hand. (Heidegger) 
17 
==PAGE:19==
This is a phenomenological reformulation of the meaning of Being, where ‘​cogito sum​’ is only a
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
branch and ‘​I am in the world​’ is the root. Another way to view this transformation is as an offering
 
   
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
of a rival metaphor of Being, an undifferentiated world of nuanced, rich, shared existence,
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
superseding the simplistic Cartesian subject-object metaphor. (Çüçen, 1998) 
It should be noted that this model is altogether hermeneutical. Considering the first mode,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
our most basic experience of other people is in skilfully engaging with them, for example in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
designing with them. Our awareness of our roles and capabilities is possible only through our
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relationships with people of other roles and capabilities. As the skilful use of a hammer is grounded
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
   
 
in a holistic understanding of a ‘technological or material whole’, so smooth interactions with
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people proceed from a ‘societal whole’. Only in society do we make sense of ourselves. (Dreyfus,
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
1991) 
The circular quality of shared experience shows up also in the second mode, of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
meaningful​, for one can isolate objects and assign them values only in light of a background of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
understanding, which is itself the result of a string of experiences shared with society. This
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding is heavily colored with tradition and dogma, unlike in the Cartesian view that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding requires ‘transparency of mind’. And even the third level exhibits social aspects:
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
scientific understanding and progress rely on conversation and consensus; scientific fact is to a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
large extent a social construct (Kuhn, 1970). 
In light of the above, the fourth level loses its significance. The isolated self seems illusive,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
suited only for rare moments of obscure philosophical pondering, not as a source of meaning.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
According to Heidegger, “​the ultimate 'ground' of intelligibility is simply shared practices​” (Dreyfus, 1991,
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
p. 157). And more generally, as tradition is woven together through history, the authenticity of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Dasein is inseparable from time (though how exactly the meaning of being is to be derived from
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
time he left as an open question, as he ran out of time) (Heidegger, 1962). 
 
​2.1.5​ Not quite. Once more.  ‘​We are in the world​ ’ 
 
Watsuji argues in ​Climate and Culture (​風土​, ​fūdo ) that while Heidegger’s phenomenological
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
framework is promising, it is far from robust; perhaps owing to his Japanese mindset, he discerned
   
     
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
a shade of Western individualism burdening that model. Heidegger’s description of authentic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
existence overemphasizes the Dasein’s rooting in time, neglecting the importance of sociality. He
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
does refer to sociality, calling it ​Mitsein​ or ​Being-with​ but this is not interactive, only a static
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
characteristic. According to Watsuji, true self resides not in the individual, nor in society; it lies in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
the intersection of these, in the emptiness resulting from their double negation. As such, his term
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for the ‘self’ (​人間, ​ningen​), which in common language means ‘human being’, is composed of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
characters meaning person and between; the self is found in the interconnection, the relationship
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
18 
==PAGE:20==
(​間柄​, ​aidagara​) amongst individuals. This relationship, the empty self, can be considered a ​sign​, akin
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
to ‘empty sounds’ within phonemes in Peirce’s ​Linguistic Structuralism (Clair, 2003). There is a notion
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
of relationship between selves in Heidegger’s system too, ​Zwischen​, but characteristically of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
either/or mentality in Western scholarship, it is polar: a contrast between a positive feature and its
 
 
 
 
     
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
absence. Such a view of betweenness naturally suggests that the self is in opposition to others,
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
feeding the worship of egoist valor discussed above. On the other hand, ​aidagara is continuous,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
enabling one to slide along it to find the point of harmony. (Clair, 2003) 
Moreover, Watsuji considers Heidegger’s notion of Being’s rootedness in time as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
insufficient, suggesting that space is equally primordial (a hint to this is the fact that the ​length of a
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
 
     
piece of time is expressed as a spatial metaphor in every language). Several concepts of space are
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
found in Heidegger’s philosophy, but none are aligned with Watsuji’s notion of a space of relations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
in which the social sign is integrated. Heidegger’s spatiality, being uncovered only when an object
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
becomes unhandy, is merely a matter of reference, association and care. The term that Watsuji
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
employs for the social space, ​fūdo , means ‘climate’ or ’topography’ in everyday use. Here the word
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
has an amplified meaning, incorporating these notions of natural environment, as well as social and
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technological culture. Watsuji argues that climate and spatial environment influence people’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
modes of being, so that their variety around the world induces a range of ethical sensitivities and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
existential beliefs. One’s view of the self and of other people is fundamentally connected to the ​fūdo
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
 
 
that one is embedded in, whence it is a driver of history. In other words, the self is contextual.
 
   
 
 
       
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
(Clair, 2003) This belief has been empirically found to be in direct contrast with those prevalent in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
egocentric societies: “​individualism implies that judgment, reasoning, and causal inference are generally
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
oriented toward the person rather than the situation or social context because the decontextualized self is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
assumed to be a stable, causal nexus … Consequently, individualism promotes a decontextualized, as opposed to
       
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
   
a situation-specific, reasoning style, one that assumes social information is not bound to social context.​”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
(Oyserman et al., 2002) 
For Descartes, existence depends only on the ego’s ​knowing​; for Heidegger, existence is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
temporal; for Watsuji, it is embedded in both time and space . Nishida, another paragon of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
2
phenomenology in the Kyoto School of Philosophy, agrees with Watsuji about the contextual self
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
but articulates it somewhat differently; he viewed ​fūdo as too passive, suggesting a more interactive
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
model inspired by William James’ notion of ​Self as Pure Experience​. (James, 1983) Namely, James
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
contended that the self is not a permanent entity à la Descartes, nor a series of feelings as Hume
 
 
 
   
   
 
     
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
proposed. Rather, it is a feeling of succession amongst a stream of thoughts which occur in contexts
 
       
   
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
that appear as felt sensations, ‘fringes of experience’. The self is thus a process, a continuous
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
replacement of the past by the present and an anticipation of the future, an emergence and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
renovation in time and the fringes of space. In Nishida’s process model too, the self is found within
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
2 Watsuji coined the term ​sonzai (​帀妰​) for existence, which is pronounced like the standard word for existence but is composed of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
characters meaning time and space respectively. 
19 
==PAGE:21==
a pure experience of time, which occurs in place (​場所​, ​basho​). While the self is empty, the fringe of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
consciousness surrounding it contains dynamic interplay between ​I and thou (​我と汝​, ​ware to nanji​).
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
It is these shifting I-thou relationships, the dialectic between self and others, that engenders
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
self-consciousness. And as with Watsuji, this self-consciousness is not individual, but between
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people in society. Moreover, unlike individualism which flows from ​eros (desire for objects), the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
I-thou relationship is rooted in ​agape (selfless love). Nishida hence also frames the true self as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Kenotic Self​, linking his model to the Christian concept of ​kenosis​, the emptying of oneself through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
unconditional love. (Clair, 2003) 
Finally, note that there is no reason to consider these concepts as mere obscure strands of
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
philosophy: they abound with potential for cross-fertilization with scientific domains such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cybernetics and neurophenomenology. For example, integrating paradigms of the Kyoto School of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Philosophy with Western phenomenology and science, Schroeder and Vallverdú have proposed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
situated phenomenology​ a holistic view of experience tying together cognitive, biological, and robotics
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
models. (Schroeder and Vallverdú, 2015) 
 
​2.1.6​ Removing the mask 
 
A sociological line of thought that is quite congruent with the Kyoto School's theories can be found
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in G.H. Mead's work, and those of his followers in the Chicago School of Sociology. (Clair, 2003) For
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
example, Mead elucidated the significance of ‘role-taking’, as one understands his/her own self only
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in relation to the roles of others. Building on this, Goffman took the metaphor of the stage to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
formulate a dramaturgical model of sociology, arguing that when interacting with others, people
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
take on a role, like an actor does. People are not completely free to choose the role themselves,
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
forcing them to act in a certain way. Therefore we have to learn to present different personas
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(social masks) depending on the context, and in a credible fashion, according to others’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expectations. Failing to do so means that we are ‘out of character’ or have the wrong script. And
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the stage tends to be an unfriendly place, where actors have to be cautious of others, playing by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
established rituals, a sense of civility. The real self is fragile and must be protected from the public.
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, if a feeling of community is attained, the need for caution diminishes, and the true self is
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
allowed to emerge, in line with Watsuji. Another prominent Chicagoan, Cooley, presented the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘primary group’ as a type of society based on love and harmony, where one is readily unmasked. In
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
such groups, even competition is constructive, performed in the same spirit that always
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
characterizes the relationships (Clair, 2003). Cooley also advocated the holistic nature of society,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explaining the interplay between all social processes: “​Our life is all one human whole ... if we cut it up
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
     
 
   
 
it dies in the process.​” (Cooley, 1964) 
 
 
20 
==PAGE:22==
​2.2​ Mental models 
 
The concept of a mental model is a powerful method in studying mental processes in design. These
 
 
   
 
     
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
are simplified representations of the world that prevent the mind’s being overloaded with data;
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more or less subconsciously constructed frameworks that allow quick gleaning and processing of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
new information (Smyth et al., 1994; Badke-Schaub et al., 2011). As such, mental models are a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
powerful tool in predicting and explaining problem-solving behavior, particularly when engaged in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
analytical-creative work (Norman, 1983). Indeed, the cognitive process of inference can be framed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as reasoning based on mental models; Hestenes (2006) has identified seven types of such reasoning
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
(eg. analysis, synthesis and abduction) which exhibit a complex interplay during creative thinking.
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
However, a precise understanding of mental model formation, and of their use in design, is yet to
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
be formulated (Badke-Schaub et al., 2011).  
Mental models are a paramount aspect of cutting-edge cognitive science. Hestenes (2006)
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presents mental models within a Modeling Theory of Cognition (Figure 3) where they, being a
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
mind’s private constructions embodied as neural networks, are distinct from Conceptual Models,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which are scientific representations of the world. These are encoded by symbols, enabling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
corresponding mental models to be activated in individual minds. As such, Mental and Conceptual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Models can be thought of simply as the subjective and objective knowledge structures, respectively,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that the human mind crafts or enhances when trying to make sense of the world. It ought to also be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
noted that Conceptual Models are transparent in their being wholly visually accessible as a symbolic
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
structure (e.g. the models of Euclidean geometry being encoded by curves and letters on the pages
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of a book), while the majority of a Mental Model often lies below the threshold of consciousness,
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and must hence be fished out by non-trivial techniques (e.g. design games, Section 2.5.3). 
 
 
Figure 3: Hestenes’ Modeling Theory of Cognition. Copied from Hestenes, 2006. 
21 
==PAGE:23==
 
Alignment between individual mental models arises from communication, resulting in a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
Shared Mental Model. Each individual’s mental model is colored by his/her knowledge and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experiences, all of which fine details have an impact of various magnitudes on the synthetic unity.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The sharing of knowledge and opinions by diverse, though mostly vocal, interactions causes each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mind to dynamically mold its mental model (Badke-Schaub and Casakin, 2015; 2017), usually
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
eventually reaching a stage of high similarity throughout the group. Again, no explanation of the
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
low-level cognitive processes behind this phenomenon has yet been proposed, but Fauconnier and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Turner’s (2002) notion of the blending of mental spaces seems a promising direction. In any case,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
such alignment has been shown to enhance the team’s coordination and performance (as is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intuitively evident). As DeChurch and Mesmer-Magnus contend, “​emergent cognition that enables team
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
members both to predict and anticipate one another’s actions and to fully utilize the often diverse array of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
expertise present in the team are essential underpinnings of team functioning.​” (DeChurch &
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Mesmer-Magnus, 2010)  
There seems to, however, be a tension between team diversity and mental model
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sharedness, at least in the initial phase of a team’s work: while heterogeneity is linked to creativity
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
and flexibility (Carpenter and Fredrickson, 2001), it hampers the formation of shared understanding
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
(Bittner and Leimeister, 2013); viewed otherwise, homogeneity induces groupthink.  
Shared Mental models have usually been allocated into one of three categories (Edmondson,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
2009):  
●
Task​: representations of the issue that is under consideration. Associated verbal activities
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
include problem definition, idea generation, explanations and elucidations, solution
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
evaluations, and decision taking. This model develops through the interactions of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
problem and solution spaces through communication, and is influenced by team dynamics. 
●
Process​: representations of procedures, strategies and rules regarding the team’s work. As
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the overall design process tends to begin in an open-ended manner, ways of organizing and
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
planning the work need to be established in order to achieve a feasible level of performance. 
●
Team​: representations of the collaboration's social facet. This is an indicator of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
collaboration motivation and the sense of belonging. Verbal actions sourcing from this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
model include rejection and appreciation of others’ ideas, confirmation as a positive
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
evaluation, and help. 
 
A major challenge in inquiries of mental models in design is the difficulty of eliciting them. Their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
behavioral manifestations are nonetheless obvious (Dong, Kleinsmann & Deken 2013) and have been
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
used most prominently in the following ways. 
Badke-Schaub and Casakin studied shared mental model formation in a team of architects
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
and clients, presenting a simple and solid methodology for mental model sharedness quantification
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
22 
==PAGE:24==
(Badke-Schaub and Casakin, 2017). A major finding is that particularly in the early stages of concept
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
generation, sharedness is a valuable coordination-simplifying asset, regardless of the shared
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mental model’s actual validity and truthfulness. Sharedness is achieved most effectively by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
emphasizing discussion belonging to the Task model (especially idea generation and solution
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
analysis), which tends to be the most common type of interaction, followed by that belonging to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
Team model (Badke-Schaub et al., 2011). However, to achieve viable coordination from the get-go,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
heterogeneous teams need not immediately engender the full set of shared mental models; a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
common design space, i.e. the Task model, suffices. 
Moreover, an important tool in the mind’s development of shared mental models is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
question-asking and feedback (Mulder, Swaak, and Kessels, 2004). Eris (2002) formulated 22
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
question classes, divided into two groups: Deep Reasoning, for understanding concepts, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Generative Design, for creating possibilities. Both types could address any of the three shared
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mental model categories above. 
Conducting an inquiry into supporting shared understanding development (in distributed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design teams) through a communication support tool based on a question-asking-feedback
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
protocol, Cash et. al (2017) found question-asking to be a critical facilitator in mental model
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
alignment. The methodology was based on concept-mapping, a tool effective in charting sharedness
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
of static knowledge like plans (Johnson and O’Connor, 2008), in other words information belonging
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to the Process model, but is deficient for tackling the dynamism the Task and Team models. It was
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
moreover found that in some cases, the improvement in shared understanding due to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intervention was perceived to be lower than it actually was, implying a need for visual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communication of such improvements in any shared mental model inter​vention. (Cash et al., 2017) 
 
In conclusion I present the following quote from a businessman’s speech: 
“​You’ve got to hang experience on a lattice work of models in your head. What are the models?
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
Well, the first rule is that you’ve got to have multiple models — because if you just have one or
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
two that you’re using, the nature of human psychology is such that you’ll torture reality so that it
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
   
fits your models, or at least you’ll think it does… So you’ve got to have multiple models. And the
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
models have to come from multiple disciplines — because all the wisdom of the world is not to be
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
     
found in one little academic department.​”
 
Munger, 1994 
 
 
 
 
23 
==PAGE:25==
​2.3​ Rhetoric 
 
In this section I lay out the philosophy and practice of rhetoric, which this project revolves around,
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
and which the succeeding Analysis and Synthesis chapters are suffused with. I begin by explaining
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
the concept of rhetoric and laying out its Aristotelian roots. Next I move onto the field’s modern
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
incarnation, followed by its connection to design. Lastly I unfold Halstrøm’s model and provide
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
examples of its use. 
 
​2.3.1​ Foundation 
 
Rhetoric is “​the faculty of observing in any given case the available means of ​persuasion​.​” (Aristotle’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Rhetoric​; Rapp, 2010) Every persuasive utterance or piece of writing, whether political manipulation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
of crowds, or a kid pleading with her parents to visit Disneyland, is a rhetorical act. In Aristotle’s
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
fundamental treatise of the subject, he deemed it a counterpoint and an analogy to dialectic: what
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dialectic is for the private or academic practice of attacking and maintaining arguments, rhetoric is
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
for the public practice of defending oneself.  
One of the core aspects of rhetoric is the ​topos​, a general argumentative form or scheme,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
based on which specific arguments can be instantiated. In line with the word’s original meaning,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘place’, the ​topoi can also be thought of as different viewpoints from which to survey an issue, or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from which to find a weak flanks to launch offensives at. Aristotle’s book ​Topics contains a list of
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
about a hundred different ​topoi for construing dialectical arguments. His book ​Rhetoric is also rife
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
with lists of ​topoi​, but instead of simply instructing dialecticians on formulating deductions, it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
focuses on the persuasiveness of public speech. Therein he also categorizes ​topoi as either
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commonplace and specific​. The former are elements of persuasion applicable in general, such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
opposites, precedent, cause and effect, time, and so on. The latter are descriptions of something
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that is good, noble, just, etc. alongside a reason for its being so. For example, ‘​Cocoa is good, for it is
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
     
pleasant​’. The commonplace hence tends to be more powerful, as it does not depend on specific
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
information that some audience members may lack, but only on general reasoning capabilities. In
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fact, the relative importance of these two is evident in the fact that the commonplace are often,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
including by the maven himself, called simply ​topoi​.  
This noble art is usually divided into three branches. ​Forensic rhetoric is used argue about
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
the past, and is thus employed particularly in judicial contexts. ​Epideictic rhetoric is used to lavish
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
its subject with praise or blame. It is best known as celebration of someone’s characteristics and
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deeds in eulogies such as obituaries and graduation speeches. Though it typically stays in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
24 
==PAGE:26==
present, while perhaps drawing from past events, its motivational power implicitly directs it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
towards the future. ​Deliberative rhetoric is used to persuade the audience to take, or refrain from,
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some action. Debate and political oratory are banal examples. (Rapp, 2010) 
Moreover, rhetorical utterances can be classified under three modes of persuasion: ​Ethos​, or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
ethical appeal, is about convincing the audience of the rhetor's credibility, that he/she is worth
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
listening to. This is achievable through for example choosing a level of vocabulary suited for the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
audience, sounding unbiased, and introducing one's expertise; ​Pathos is emotional appeal, achieved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
via emotional stories, tone of voice, and so on; ​Logos is the appeal to logic, by e.g. referring to facts,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
using analogies, citing certain authorities, and constructing logical arguments. 
 
​2.3.2​ Modernity 
 
Although Aristotle’s ​Rhetoric had an enormous influence on classical authors and orators such as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Cicero and Quintilian, it was interpreted only through a pragmatic and historical lens, and its
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
philosophically salient aspects were neglected. (Rapp, 2010) And remarkably, it was not rediscovered
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
during the Renaissance alongside other classics, but had to wait all the way until the 1960s for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
appreciation to bud. The field was resuscitated as New Rhetoric, with a shifted aim and amplified
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
canon. (Enos and Brown, 1994) In contrast to the old focus on molding formal and aesthetic features
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of oral or written text, and fine-tuning them to perfection in order to manipulate an audience to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conviction, the modern theories are about discourse as ​action​: how writing or speech can be used to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
enlighten, persuade, amuse and inspire people, regardless of the form and scope of discourse (Enos,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
1996): “​The speaker seeks to provide the audience with the reasons for adopting a new attitude or taking a new
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
course of action. In this sense, rhetoric is an art of shaping society, changing the course of individuals and
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
communities, and setting patterns for new action.​” (Buchanan, 1985) It should be noted that the term
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
New Rhetoric is not a theory in itself, but covers diverse ways of handling the transition into
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
modernity, some with little in common. (Lampe, 2010) The discussion here concentrates on the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
strands that are most relevant to design. 
The contemporary theorist McKeon (1989) has explored the potential of rhetoric as a system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
of guidance for innovation; he argues for generalizing the art from mere dialectics and oration to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
uncovering and creating value: “​We need a new art of invention and discovery in which places are used as
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
means by which to light up modes and meanings of works of art and natural occurrences and to open up
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aspects and connections in existence and possibility.​” (McKeon, 1989) In his essay ​Creativity and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Commonplace​, he refutes the tendency to deem the commonplace ​topoi as a list of prior knowledge,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
arguing that they are seeds of invention, “​places for the perception, discovery and explanation of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
unknown … ​the sources of new perceptions operative in new directions in the thought and culture and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
philosophy of the twentieth century​” (and probably for this century too). The ground for this is the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
25 
==PAGE:27==
capacity of the commonplace to serve as a meeting place for the familiar and the unfamiliar,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enabling concepts, structures and doctrines to be recombined in an exploratory spirit (McKeon,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
1989; Miller, 2016). The common topoi therefore serve both as formal checklists and as novelty
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
catalyzers.  
In fact, Aristotle’s ​Physics suggests them to be like vessels in which form and matter interact
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
to yield matter that is shaped for persuasion. He notes that the special ​topoi lack such a fruitful
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
potential, as they can accommodate only one kind of notion, like a place allocated on a shelf for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
Nth volume of an encyclopedia, so that they are useful only as sources of preexisting arguments.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, Miller offers examples of technical innovation using ​topoi that are specific to a particular
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
field of argument, yet able to identify multiple pre-existing proofs. The ‘common’ and the ‘specific’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ought thus to be taken as relative, not absolute, characteristics. (Miller, 2016) 
 
​2.3.3​ Design 
 
“​is the art of shaping arguments about the artificial or human-made world, arguments which may
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
be carried forward in the concrete activities of production in each of these areas, with objective
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
 
results ultimately judged by individuals, groups, and society.​” (Buchanan, 1995, p. 46) 
   
Unlike in the traditional dialectical view of the design process, where it is rendered as a game of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
   
 
 
argumentation between a proponent and an opponent (which can be oneself too) in order to test
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
out ideas, New Rhetoric allows it to be considered as a social interaction undergirded by persuasion
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
between an arguer and an audience, which frequently switch positions. Here the validity of an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
argument does not depend on objective facts, but on subjective concepts, on the audience’s shared
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
background of experience and beliefs. The arguer has to simply make sure that any argument is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
based on a premise that the audience deems valid. (Stumpf and McDonnell, 2002) 
It is therefore evident that fundamental rhetorical frameworks need to be established for
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design in order to make the most of its potential as a persuasive art. Although such overarching
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
frameworks are yet to be fully formulated, Halstrøm offers a highly promising basis. Before
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
examining its practical value, it is worthwhile to get a grasp of the general reconceptualization of
 
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
the design process it implies. 
Buchanan stated that “​the designer, instead of simply making an object or thing, is actually creating
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
a persuasive argument that comes to life whenever a user considers or uses a product as a means to some end​”
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
   
     
   
 
 
(Buchanan, 1985). Despite this statement being coherent with all of the above elucidation, it is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
somewhat misleading, for it implies that the rhetor-designer only has to find out what the audience
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
wants in order to be able to figure out the best path of persuasion. However, the audience does not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
always exist. As Verganti (2009) expounds, and as Steve Jobs has notoriously showcased, people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
26 
==PAGE:28==
sometimes know not what they yearn. For example, people used to superficially desire faster
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
horses, while deep down they wanted simply more efficient transportation, which we finally got via
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
industrialization. Verganti contends for a distinction between ‘radical technological innovation’ and
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘radical innovation of meaning’, otherwise known as ‘design-driven innovation’, arguing that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
latter holds neglected potential (Verganti, 2009). Many milestones of design that are etched into the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
industrialized world’s collective memory, such as iPhone and Wii, which comprised already existing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technologies, were eagerly embraced by masses who beforehand had not the faintest idea of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
passion that those designs would foster in them. 
Hence an enormous conundrum emerges from its lair: how to evaluate an artefact's appeal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to its potential users? The audience in ancient Greece was the people gathered in the agora, lapping
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
up aural content of a range of eloquences and various levels of truth and persuasion. “​Aristotle and
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Cicero consider audiences purely as something given​” (Burke, 1969, p. 64). However, contemporary mass
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
media has to be tailored to deal with the reality that their audiences are far from well-defined. As
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
such, the task of rhetoric is nowadays not only to convince an audience, but to constitute it too.
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
This paradigm augmentation in design can be modeled as a triple co-evolution of problem, solution
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
and audience spaces (Halstrøm, 2015). The cornerstone of molding out the audience is ​identification​:
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
“​You persuade a man only insofar as you can talk his language by speech, gesture, tonality, order, image,
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
attitude, idea, identifying your ways with his​” (Burke, 1969, p. 55). The process of making audience
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
members enter the subject position that they are offered is not one of persuasion, but of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
identification, or recognition of one’s own reflection in a mirror that the rhetor construes of words,
 
 
   
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
   
 
gestures, sartorial style, music, images etc. For example, if you are addressed as a ‘designer’ and you
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
accept that subject position, then you are already persuaded of some set of values that you associate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
with designers, subscribing to an implicit narrative (a common one is the heroism, acting as a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
conduit between society and a transcendental realm, as detailed in Section 2.1.3). 
 
​2.3.4​ In practice
