Halstrøm bases his practical triple co-evolution framework on the nature of rhetoric as an art of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
invention, through ​topoi​. The framework can be used in two modes, ​epideictic and ​deliberative​, as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expounded below. Despite the thoroughness of Aristotle’s and Cicero’s lists of ​topoi​, Halstrøm found
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
their expansiveness to render them too unwieldy for use in design. He thus adopts Hermogenes’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
two sets of six ​topoi​, which the venerable thinker curated so as to maximize their potential for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
amplification of persuasion. At least one ​topos is combined from each set and thrown into a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
designer’s right-side brain to yield a variety of arguments: 
27 
==PAGE:29==
 
●
Circumstance topoi​: time (​khronos​), place (​topos​), person (​prosôpon ), way (​tropos​), act (​prâgma ),
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cause (​aition​). 
●
Parallel topoi​: comparison (​parabolê ), example (​paradeigma​), minor (​mikroteron​), greater
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(​meizôn ), equal (​isos​), opposite (​enantion​). 
 
 
 
Epideictic 
 
The first mode of the framework is the application of these ​topoi to discovering diverse ways of
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
persuasion whereby the artefact (not necessarily physical) can be designed. In other words, it is a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
       
source of viewpoints from which to consider the artefact’s object of celebration. For example,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
imagine designing a cafe for Disneyland. Of the myriad culinary establishments in the park, this
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
one is supposed to be themed on classic Mickey Mouse films, yet suffused with a classy mood; it
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
should persuade the visitors of the value of those cinematic treasures. The process could go as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
follows, beginning with eg. ​place​ time​ and ​equal​: 
●
You decide to exhibit Mickey’s evolution over the decades (​time​) in the ​place by making the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
building’s cross-section circular, with the counter and kitchen in the middle, and a picture
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
of each of the variants in Figure 4 distributed evenly around the walls, the first and last also
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
shaking
hands
over
the
front
door
to
emphasize
the
character’s
​time​lessness
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(​comparison​/​equal​). 
●
Preferring exquisite wood and stone over artificial materials (​place​), you present the cafe's
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
theme in a rich, wholesome aesthetic and tactile context devoid of bright colors (​minor​),
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inducing the customers to associate the two intuitively different conceptual worlds with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
each other (​opposite/equal​). 
And so on. When these ​topoi​ are exhausted, you could move onto emphasizing ​minor​: 
●
You try to reinforce the high-class spirit by minimizing (​minor​) the amount of children
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(​person​) by setting a relatively small (​minor​) table size to make the experience unfavorable for
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
families with more than one child (​greater/cause​).  
●
Next you consider the ​way you present the characters in light of the need for a classy,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
non-trite mood where the audience does not feel overwhelmed with the theme. Thus you
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decide to minimize (​minor​) the explicit display of characters in favor of suggestive forms,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
more subtly playful design (eg. a table in the shape shown in Figure 5).   
In this way each ​topos​ helps to tease out concepts for further amplifying the design’s claim.  
   
 
28 
==PAGE:30==
 
Deliberative 
 
Secondly, the ​topoi ease deliberation of designers’ process choices and solution, enabling them to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
persuade colleagues, clients and themselves of the value of designing in a certain way. This is close
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
   
 
to Hermogenes’ own application of the method, in a verbal form. Each of the four bullet-pointed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
examples above incorporate some this mode too in their argumentation, but may have to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expanded with further ​topoi in a real case of e.g. presenting a sketch of the design to Disneyland’s
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
executives. For example, someone may be unconvinced by the last idea, deeming it natural that in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
addition to the Mickeys on the walls, there should be some place where classic films are projected
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
onto some surface, enabling customers to experience them directly. To convince this executive of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
your choice, you could emphasize the target customers’ (​person​) mindset (​way of thinking), saying
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that it would be an unnecessary distraction as customers usually want to concentrate on eating and
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
socialization (​act​). You could then think of the whole ​place and perform a ​comparison​, highlighting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
the redundancy of the executive’s idea with reference to the house on Disneyland’s main street
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
where classics are shown, and suggesting that this cafe’s aim is not to rival but to be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complementary to that place, offering the customers’ minds freedom to contemplate on the themes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
in their own ​ways​. 
 
 
        Figure 4: Mickey’s evolution.
Figure 5: An abstract furniture 
 
shape alternative to  
Mickey’s silhouette.  
 
Moreover, Hermogenes’ method can also be used to discover an abundance of viewpoints
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
for molding out user research techniques. For example, when performing observations in a cafe,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
you could first consider ​act and ​time​, coming up with the idea to examine how small talk (​minor/act​)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
29 
==PAGE:31==
changes throughout the day; then finding out how various types (​way​) of latte art (Figure 6) affect
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
people’s behavior (​act​); does it occasion (​cause​) the topic of conversation to veer onto something
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
particular; how is this different between (​comparison​) regulars and new customers (​person​); and so
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on. Question formulation is also made easier; with the intention of discovering how latte art affects
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
a cafe's experience, you could explore the audience space by ​example, cause and way​: “How did you
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decide (​way​/​cause​) to order that? Please describe (​example​) the mood here; what do you think are the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
greatest factors (​cause​) in it? Could you sketch (​way/example​) some of your topics of conversation?”
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
So the method is evidently useful throughout the design process, from research through synthesis,
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
all the way to evaluation. 
 
 
Figure 6: Different types of latte art. 
​ 
2.4​ Metaphors 
 
Symbolic representation of reality, embodied in language and metaphors, is an integral rhetorical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
element, and seems to be a promising concept by which to gain further insight into shared
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding. A metaphor indicates a shared way of perceiving a situation and the degree to
   
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
which one can affect it. As such, it can unconsciously foster a sense of performance, and a feeling of
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
   
   
success or failure. Tommelleri et al. (2015) categorized the metaphors used by medical professionals
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in a hospital into ​apparatus​, ​family and ​team​; the apparatus metaphor suggests an organization
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
governed by impersonal rules, and that social interactions should also be formal, controlled by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
functional principles; the family metaphor tends to reinforce a feeling of amity between people
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
within a ward (akin to Cooley’s ‘primary group’, Section 2.1.6), but induce an overly defensive
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
attitude towards other wards; and the team metaphor is associated with a unified sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
problem-solving and mission between heterogeneous professionals. Similarly, Roldan Riejos et al.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(2010) expounded on the daily use of metaphors amongst architects and engineers in the context of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
hospital design. They found the most common type of metaphor to be that of ​the hospital as patient
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
and the designers as doctors​, entailing the treating of architectural pathologies (perhaps invoking the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
30 
==PAGE:32==
image of the savior-designer discussed in Section 2.1.3). The authors identified two categories of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
metaphor: 
1.
Image. These simply project of the schema of a mental image over another. For example, ‘​the
 
 
 
   
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospital is a brain’​. 
2.
Conceptual. These constitute complete semantic fields, triggering numerous associations.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
For example, ‘​the hospital is an intelligent being​’ brings up not only the concept of a brain, but
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
also senses, nervous system, cardiovascular system etc. 
 
 
In design, metaphors direct focus, and the identification of difference. They are lenses through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which the designer sees his/her task, prompting actions that modify the design situation and vice
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
versa. The designer’s changing experience, from which these metaphors emerge onto the limelight
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the consciousness, composes hence a hermeneutical system with the situation; they can be
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considered forms of ‘pre-understanding’ that designers project into the world in order to enter a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
3
constructive dialogue with the task (Coyne and Snodgrass, 1996), a “​dialectic
of guessing and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
4
validation​.” (Ricoeur, 1973). The metaphors prevailing in a team tend to shift as members reflect on
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the situation and present differing restatements of the problem and the process. This tendency is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
in effect a natural version of Dorst’s ​Frame creation (Dorst et al., 2016). “​How are designers driven from
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
one metaphoric orientation to another? The entailments of privilege empower the design process. ... The power
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
of particular metaphors lies in the complex web of relations in which particular metaphors have use. Certain
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
metaphors are favored over others (for particular situations) by virtue of our historical situation, the norms of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
our practice as professionals, the conventions of the design studio, and the personal preoccupations of teachers
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
and colleagues.​” (Coyne, Snodgrass and Martin, 1994) This web of relations could be hypothesized to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be a dimension of ​fūdo (Section 2.1.5), so that it may have a significant influence on the attainment
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
of the sweet spot on the ego-world continuum. 
Coyne, Snodgrass and Martin (1994) provide a set of metaphor types prevalent amongst
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
architectural students. If it is valid to extrapolate from those findings, then it is obvious that in the
 
       
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
   
 
context of design, metaphors are fairly evenly mixed between the rationalist and Romantic camps. I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
have already touched upon the latter tendency in Section 2.1.3: for example, the result of design is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
seen as a work of art, and the progress of a design task as a personal journey, a mission of
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
self-expression and discovery. Chaos, and the excitement and promises of ambiguity are commonly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
glorified. This mindset entails reflection on one’s feelings towards the process and the result. On
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3 ​The word ‘project’ originally meant ‘throwing before’ (as did ‘forecast’ and ‘problem’). This is may have been a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
metaphor for design’s hermeneutical nature, for designers begin a task with a vague pre-understanding of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
solution: the meaning of the whole is anticipated, and then the individual parts are explicated, enabling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
re-determination of the whole, and so the cycle continues. By constant revisions arises understanding. (Ormiston
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and Schrift,​ op. cit.​, 1990, p. 130 (fn. 6)) 
4 ​Hence a designer can be said to swim in two parallel interactive flows: a dialectical one with the task and a
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
rhetorical one with oneself and team members. 
31 
==PAGE:33==
the other hand, rationalist metaphors are about detachment and analysis; design is seen as a logical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
procedure, so that systematic striving for excellence is possible by applying the correct methods.
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
There sometimes appears conflict between these two orientations in a designer’s mind, and a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
metaphor belonging to one is occasionally used to justify that approach against the other. Thus in
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
groupwork it may be useful to disclose the metaphoric nature of design-related thinking in order to
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
mend the team’s spirit in response to ‘breakdowns’ (ie. when ​unhandiness occurs, Section 2.1.4) like
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stark conflict, slow progress, or searing criticism; for example, objectifying the language of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reflection could help to dissolve feelings of personal inadequacy, and subjectifying it could coax
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
overly stubborn members to loosen their grip on issues they view as fundamental. 
Metaphors are therefore not simply poetical of images and similes, but powerful linguistics
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tools that forge social relationships, align mental models, and lubricate creativity.  
Even more generally, metaphors are not only linguistic elements: “​The word simply refers to a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
dynamic whereby we transport any kind of content or meaning—visual, sensory, auditory, linguistic,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
proprioceptive, or any combination thereof—from one place or thing to another. Etymologically the word
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
“metaphor” derives from two ancient Greek words, ​meta​, which means to move over, across, and ​phoreo​,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which means to carry over, and these roots indicate that metaphors are cognitive devices that people use in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
many media and forms.​” (Goldhagen, 2017)  
The nonconscious cognition that occurs in us while navigating our environments come in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
two forms. Firstly, direct responses, which are completely innate and physiological; some “​feature in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
the environment ​in and of itself provokes in us a rapid, automatic response​”, such as cool and less
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
saturated colors calming us; the smell of rot disgusting us; the various stimuli implemented into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
haunted house rides provoking our fear. Then there are indirect responses, originating in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mental models that we accumulate throughout our years of inhabiting environments. Examples are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
when glimpsing a radiant shore in summer makes us want to plunge into the sea; when viewing the
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
school’s front door or gate made us long to escape the institution; when we estimate the distance to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
the grocery shop by an mental model of our neighborhood. (Goldhagen, 2017) 
 
 
Figure 7: Beijing’s National Aquatics Centre and London Aquatics Centre, despite their obvious materiality evoking rich
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
associations with the playfulness and flow of water. 
 
32 
==PAGE:34==
A major category of indirect responses is embodied metaphors. Consider the bubbly walls,
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
made of steel and plastic, of Beijing’s National Aquatics Centre; and the wavy profiles of London
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Aquatics Centre, made of concrete (Figure 7). Their materiality renders to them an obviously
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
constructed affect. Yet these structures “​stir up a cascade of associations that are logically unconnected to
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
   
their physicality​” (Goldhagen, 2017): they convey feelings of fluidity and playfulness, something of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
experience of being inside them. Note also that they have been designed (whether un- or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
consciously) to downplay water’s negative attributes: coldness, wetness and danger. Another
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
example is the practice of saying ‘​I feel at home​’ in a foreign house. The strength of the association of
   
 
   
   
   
     
 
 
 
   
 
   
‘home’ with qualities like coziness, safety and belonging is evidenced in their being encompassed by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
the word ‘homely’ , and its equivalents in numerous other languages. Yet another extremely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
5
common instance is the exploitation of our primordial ‘​important is big; substantial is weighty​’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
metaphor, which we gained by our experience of interacting with our caretakers in our age of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
smallness: “​People’s associations of physical scale and size with social power informs how architects design
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
even less august buildings. Through proportion and design, even the smallest of buildings can impart a sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
august gravity​” (eg. Kahn and Tyng’s bathhouse in Trenton, Figure 8). And musing on Eisenman’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
failure to design a memorial that conveys the horror of genocide, as described in Section 1.2.2, it is
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
     
easy to recognize that “​his attempt to spatially manifest the metaphor ​‘oppressively repetitive’ ​lapses into
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
its semantic opposite: ​‘reassuringly patterned.’​”​  (Goldhagen, 2017) 
In other words, the huge set of mental models called metaphors is relevant not only for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
sake of the design process, but for the result of the process too. Indeed, the use of metaphors being
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
a subset of rhetoric, this truth is encapsulated more generally in Halstrøm’s work (2017), where he
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
makes evident the necessity for imbuing both the design process and its resulting artefact with rich
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
persuasion, easing the designer’s workflow, and a flourishing of a variety of values for the artefact’s
 
 
 
 
 
   
     
   
 
 
 
 
users. As such, it is obvious that I should try to integrate metaphors into the foregoing project. 
 
 
Figure 8: Trenton Bath House. Kahn and Tyng exploited the ‘​substantial is weighty​’ metaphor, monumentalizing a small 
building by exaggerating its weight and suppressing external markers of scale. (Goldhagen, 2017) 
5 For example, in The Hobbit, Elrond’s house is called ‘​The Last Homely House East of the Sea’​, and is described elsewhere 
as “​a refuge for the weary and the oppressed, and a treasury of good counsel and wise lore.​” 
33 
==PAGE:35==
​2.5​ Design games 
 
In light of my decision to make the intervention into a design game, whereof reason is given in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
introduction of Chapter 4, I retrofitted this section here.  
To gain a profound understanding of the theory behind design games, I refer to an
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
authoritative opus on this topic, Vaajakallio’s thesis ​Design games as a tool, a mindset and a structure
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
   
 
   
(Vaajakallio, 2012), ​from which the knowledge in the following section is wholly drawn, unless
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
otherwise referenced. Therein can be found a treasure trove of information highly relevant for
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
anyone intending to craft any kind of social and interactive tool for engendering shared mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
models, as evidenced by the following familiar-sounding quote from there: “​It has been proposed that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to build up successful co-design gatherings and the project as a whole, it is essential to explicate stakeholders’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
     
   
 
 
concerns early enough to accommodate various interests​”. 
It ought to be noted that the term ‘design game’ is supple and context-specific, and there is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
yet no scholarly definition that would adequately cover all the types of activity that one tends to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intuitively deem design games. Yet for the purposes of this applied project, I propose the following
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
definition: ​An activity embedded into a design process, engaging multiple people into defining or filling the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
problem, solution and audience spaces by exploiting playfulness.  
 
​2.5.1​ Aims 
Within co-creation gatherings, design games can be said to have the following three main purposes: 
1.
Creating a common design language​. ​Engaging players in intensive sharing of opinions and
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concepts builds up mutual empathic understanding. Using ambiguous and fragmented game
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
material causes participants to make explicit their choices and understanding, besides
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
giving space for personal ideas. 
2.
Nurturing a creative and explorative attitude​. Tangible material thaws minds that have been
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
frozen into futility by everyday life. The rules and the material promote diverse expressions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of one’s ideas, beliefs and emotions: writing, building, acting etc.
 
3.
Facilitating the envisioning of possibilities​. Establishing an interplay between what exists and
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what could exist. Design games are conduits for constructive interweaving of players’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
everyday knowledge with the ambiguity of future. In addition, the players are can be cast
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
into a storytelling mode, where several people’s experiences augment each other in order to
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
yield coherent scenarios. 
  
34 
==PAGE:36==
 
​2.5.2​ Fundamental criteria
 
 
In order for a design game to be fruitful, it has to be pervaded by ​play spirit​: an immersive playful
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and explorative mindset that prompts the taking of risks and the bearing of uncertainty; an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
attitude of relaxation and mindfulness, of heedful ​hygge , where the mind readily flies between
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6
intuition and rational thought, and spontaneity abounds. In order to establish play spirit, a game
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
has to have three critical qualities: 
 
1.
Magic circle​. An ideal playground, free from customs and mores of everyday life, induces
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
probing of the consequences of alternative visions; exploration between the real and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
imagined. 
 
2.
Sovereign space-time domain​. Space is transmutable throughout play. A mixture of transitions
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
in event time, set time and symbolic time induces movement between past, present and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
future experiences. 
3.
Rule-freedom equilibrium​. Threads of creativity emanating from each participant are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
efficiently nurtured by rules crafted to maintain a balance between the correct direction of
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
play, and a maximally fertile coupling between the threads in order to produce ideas high in
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
quantity (and sometimes also quality).  
 
​2.5.3​ Knowledge levels 
 
As the structure of knowledge in a human mind is far from monolithic, it is beneficial to survey the
 
 
 
 
     
 
   
 
 
     
   
 
 
particular methods that are best suited for prompting each type of information to emerge from its
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
lair. Sanders and Dandavate (1996) propose four types of knowledge levels: explicit, observable, tacit
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
and latent (Figure 9). The explicit is information that people convey to others verbally when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
questioned, for example in interviews. The next type, observable, is the data that is embedded in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
practices and actions. The last two are usually unconscious, requiring more subtle techniques to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
lure them out, for they are composed of tacit knowledge as well as the fuzzy stuff: dreams and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
feelings. A design game has to thus be carefully designed to act as an efficient conductor of those
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
6 ​Hygge​ is a somewhat ambiguous term often associated with comfort and coziness, particularly in the context of indoor 
environments, but its general meaning is a feeling that is special or charming, conducive of the flow state. 
35 
==PAGE:37==
elusive things, transferring them from the expert’s mind onto others, or in this project’s case, from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
a mind seeped in one kind professional background to another seeped in a different one.  
Note that one category of such techniques is the use of metaphors, due to their enormous
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
associative power, and their often primordial quality: “​our most resonant metaphors originate in our
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
early childhood experiences​” (Goldhagen, 2017). In other words, many of our embodied metaphors were
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
seeded by or with our most fundamental dreams and fears. 
 
 
Figure 9: The best approaches for eliciting from each knowledge level, according to Sanders & Dandavate. 
Copied from Vaajakallio. 
 
Hence, the best design games aimed at eliciting information from users are comprised of a holistic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
method, enabling the extraction of knowledge from all four levels. In other words, in general not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
only ought the players be made to create ideas, but they should also be verbally questioned during
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the process . However, unlike for the later stage of Integrated Design-Build where the architects
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
7
desire more detailed information from the users (particularly in complex healthcare projects with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
much department-specific knowledge), the focus of the foregoing project is not on extracting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
deeper kinds data as such: though that too is a significant aspect that should be given
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
consideration, the problem space calls rather for a thoroughness of ​topos​-driven deliberation,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
coupled with a rapid flourishing of shared understanding, as crystallized in the two outcomes in
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Section 1.3.1. There is therefore no need for instructing the facilitator of the game resulting from
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
this project to observe the players’ body language and analyze their utterances in depth, for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
explicit information will likely suffice at this point, particularly as not all of the intended audience
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
will be the Integrated Design-Build project’s users; the architects, engineers and design managers
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tend to not have many dreams and feelings about the context, with which they most often lack
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
intimate familiarity. Yet if there is a way to imbue the solution with an inherent potential to
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
catalyze the frothing forth of a user’s unconsciousness, it should by all means be amplified. 
7 Note that the most effective method, entering people’s dreams and performing an extraction as in the film ​
, 
is yet to be developed, and its connection with the design process is yet to be formed. 
36 
==PAGE:38==
 
​2.5.4​ Boundary objects 
 
Vaajakallio uses the terms ‘design materials’ and ‘props’ to refer to the artefacts that are employed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
in early concept design, which “​should be rather anonymous and open for new interpretations.​” These are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
either ‘building blocks’ that the participants use to create mockups, i.e. tangible representations of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
their discussion, or fragments of user data that enable insights into the problem and audience
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
spaces. As the form and meaning of the design materials are not initially fixed, they are distinct
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from properly developed prototypes. The best design materials used to prompt the imagination are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
ones that strike a balance between concreteness and abstraction: they should be detailed enough to
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
support easy communication, yet ambiguous enough to warrant freedom of creativity.  
Tangible props function also as ​things-to-think-with​. They, in combination with the hands
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
manipulating them, serve as an external repository of cognitive power. By outsourcing some of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
thinking from the mind to the world, not only is the participant’s communicative effectiveness
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
increased, but mental capacity is freed for memory (and similarly for mere gesturing, as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
highlighted in Section 5.2.3). Tangible props are especially useful for representatives of envisioned
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users, for “​a ​trained designer may use a pen and a piece of paper to illustrate his ideas while other
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stakeholders need other kinds of design material to be able to sketch​”. (Johansson, 2005, p 17) 
 
Design materials are a subset of ​boundary objects​, which are “​those objects that both inhabit several
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
communities of practice and satisfy the informational requirements of each of them. Boundary objects are thus
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
both plastic enough to adapt to local needs and constraints of the several parties employing them, yet robust
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enough to maintain a common identity across sites​” (Bowker & Star, 1999, p. 297). Other kinds of
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
boundary object are for example sketches, techniques, stories, metaphors and memories.  
Bucciarelli (2002) contends that professionals inhabit different ‘object worlds’ depending on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their disciplinary expertise, causing them to see the same object of design differently. Furthermore,
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
they speak different ‘languages’. As such, they need to create various artefacts, such as sketches,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
models and acronyms, to serve as lubricants of social interaction and transformers of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding from one object world to another: “​These artifacts, as boundary objects, provide a milieu
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
for inquisition and exploration of the whole and its interfaces within, as well serving to illustrate hard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technical features or function when deployed by participants from different object worlds.​” (Bucciarelli, 2002)
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
As such, the selection of boundary objects is critical when designing a design game if its power of
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
cross-disciplinary mental model alignment is to be maximized. And they are even exploited ​within
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
object worlds (e.g. between architects) to dilute analytic thought into more volatile substance,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conducive to creativity.  
 
37 
==PAGE:39==
​2.5.5​ Enlightening examples 
 
Design with Intent 
 
The aim of Lockton’s Design with Intent toolkit is to help designers to ideate products, services and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
   
 
 
 
 
environments that foster social and environmental benefits by influencing their users’ behavior
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Lockton, 2013). Its core is composed of a sizeable set of techniques for comprehending and
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
changing behavior, drawn from diverse psychological and technical disciplines. With this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
card-deck-based system, designers can find the most appropriate subset of their solution space
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
given a behavior-related problem space. Note that inspirational material such as card decks are a
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
common element of design workshops​ (Davis, 2010; Golembewski and Selby, 2010; Clatworthy, 2011)​  
Lockton created the toolkit, which was based on the Ideo framework, by iterative
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
development and testing with design practitioners and students. This methodology belongs to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domain of action research, where the design process follows a ​plan-act-observe-reflect approach
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
(Robson, 1993). It should be noted that in the ‘act’ stage the researcher embeds an artefact and a
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
procedure into workshops in an attempt to change the way that the participants behave, but does
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not him/herself contribute to the ideation. “​The author facilitated the sessions and ‘acted' in the sense of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
introducing the toolkit but unlike some variants of `workshop' practice, did not take part in any presentation or
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
‘teaching' elements beyond introducing the procedures, answering clarification questions and discussing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ideas generated by the participants afterwards.​” (Lockton, 2013) 
Lockton’s thesis contains a wide review of toolkits and idea generation techniques that
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would be useful for anyone trying to structure a new toolkit or design game. The following list is a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
sampling of apparently highly effective mechanics in the final version of Design with Intent that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Lockton gleaned from that treasure trove of knowledge and from the tests: 
●
The players go through all of the clusters of cards, finding out whether any of them
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
inspires any concepts in the solution space. 
●
Groups should first discuss their ideas pairwise, then ‘report back’ to the whole
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
group. 
●
A great creativity booster is choosing two random cards and trying to think of ways
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
to apply the techniques described thereon together onto the problem. 
●
Each workshop should conclude with each group crystallizing its ‘best’ concepts to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
every other group in the room. This can be conducted verbally, by sketches, or by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
performance, perhaps with props. 
●
Early users made use of the toolkit in a variety of ways: not only individual and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
group brainstorming, but also informal inspiration, browsing through the deck like
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
38 
==PAGE:40==
a book, classification of existing ideas, etc. As such, the toolkit was structured so as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to be easy to apply in many ways. 
 
All of these insights seem promising in the context of this project too. A comprehensive list is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
compiled in Lockton’s thesis. (Lockton, 2013) 
 
 
A storytelling game 
 
The core of designing can be framed as the envisioning of possible futures. A common way to go
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
about this is storytelling, the creation of more or less coherent sets of scenarios. The benefit of
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
scenarios is that they are both specific and flexible, enabling the ambiguity of the design situation
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to be harnessed. Though describing a particular interpretation of a use situation, a scenario’s
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
deliberate incompleteness makes it open for change and negotiation. (Carroll, 2000) Storytelling is
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
particularly beneficial for fostering empathy for the users, forming a shared mental model of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
subjective use experience by making the players ‘walk a while in a user’s shoes’. Explicitly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
character-oriented games are undergirded by a similar methodology, resulting in a deep persona,
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
as opposed to those focused on building scenarios or stories. However, in most cases, immersion
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
into a role is integrated into the creation of a narrative. 
A major strand of these kinds of games are ones where the scenarios are enacted, usually
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with props. Some designers/researchers prefer to situate the enactment in constructed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
environments; others make professional actors perform the scenarios; and yet others let existing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
users enact them in their own environments (Brandt, 2006). And while the tangible props are often
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sufficient for establishing the play spirit, as many people have no experience of such enactment,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
there is typically a need for a warmup . A comprehensive survey of the theory of drama-based
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
8
workshops is presented by Vaajakallio. 
 
Vaajakallio documented the use of a character-oriented storytelling game conducted with the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
people-flow company KONE, with the purpose of gaining new insights about seniors for the R&D
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
department, and finding new B2B opportunities in the context of senior homes.   
Knowledge of the difficulty of encouraging players to act (i.e. perform dramatically) led the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
game’s designers to opt for ​tabletop role-playing, which demands less bodily engagement than
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
physical role immersion. The players sat around a table and verbally acted out a story from certain
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
characters’ perspectives. Boundary objects included character templates, photos and quotations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from users of senior houses, and a senior house weekly schedule. In fact, the whole concept of the
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
8 “​[I]t would be more appropriate to describe the first part of the workshop as ​tune-up​ instead of ​warm-up​, since the aim is not so much about 
warming the body for the exercises than tuning-in the participants to each other and to a right mood. Correspondingly, in co-design, ​warm-up​ is 
often also called ​sensitizing​, since it aims at gradually transforming participants’ thoughts into the topic of the co-design gathering and towards a 
creative mood.​” (Vaajakallio, 2012, p.67) 
39 
==PAGE:41==
senior house can be seen as a boundary object, as it was used mainly as the context for finding new
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
partnership opportunities between KONE and representatives of two other companies (seniors were
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
not actually invited to play). The participants were divided into four groups of four, with an even
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
spread of professional backgrounds. 
Rather than being structured by game rules, the fragmented field data was used for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
free-form ideation to come up with scenarios. This fairly fuzzy process was successful, resulting in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
several themes that were deemed important for senior houses, as well as some ​design drivers
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Wikberg & Keinonen, 2000, pp 193-206) that should be used to direct the designing later on. One of
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
the critical personal discoveries that was made was the distinct needs and values of each senior,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
leading to consideration of eg. modular and personalized service solutions. Furthermore, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
role-playing approach, requiring interaction between the players, directed the discussion towards
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the social facet of seniors’ lives, triggering topics such as the ease of visiting their rooms and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
collaboration between several buildings. This game was then later played at KONE’s R&D
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
department too, building on the themes found here. (Vaajakallio, 2012) 
 
40 
==PAGE:42==
​2.6​ Summary 
 
In this chapter I presented various fields of knowledge from which I will draw out the strands of
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
knowledge needed for reaching my aim. The first section elucidates the philosophical foundation of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
individualism, arguing for its fallacy and presenting a phenomenological view of the criticality of
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
social interaction in forming the Self. This body of knowledge could holistically inform my design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decisions later on to balance its individualistic and collectivistic aspects, amplifying the fruitful
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
relationship between the participants. 
The mental model section gives an outline of their theory and practice, in particular their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
use in design research. My first objective being the alignment of the Integrated Design-Build
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stakeholders’ mental models of their expectations, these theories shall suffuse my entire analytic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and design process, including the intervention’s evaluation. For example, I will focus on the Task
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
model, omitting the Process and Team models (for reasons given in Section 3.1.2). 
The rhetoric section describes the field’s roots in antiquity and its modern manifestation,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
arguing for its importance in design as a tool for innovation. The section concludes with an
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
overview of Halstrøm’s rhetorical-design framework, providing concrete examples of using it to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
conceptualize persuasive designs. This framework will not only serve to inspire me, but I shall
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
altogether base my solution’s structure on it, and use it to strongly inform the answers to my
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
second and third research questions. 
The section about metaphors outlines the general patterns of metaphor use in architecture,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
particularly in terms of embodied cognition. For example, architects frequently talk of designing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospitals in terms of a patient-doctor relationship, or of hospitals being intelligent beings with
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
various organ systems, while nurses tend to view their organization as e.g. a family or a team. All
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
the evidence gathered there makes it clear that I ought to endow my solution with metaphors that
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would help the participants to rise above the level of excessive specificity when considering their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design tasks, and to boost their ability of viewing their aims from a multitude of angles. 
The final section, a review of design game theory, is a summary of Vaajakallio’s ​magnum opus
 
 
   
   
 
 
     
   
 
 
 
of the field. It includes the foundational criteria that any design game should fulfill, a model for
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
eliciting every type of knowledge from participants, and guidelines for boundary objects. These are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
critical strands of wisdom that I will apply to both the conceptual structure and the physical
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
manifestation of the intervention. In this section I also included two examples of design games that
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
are highly relevant to this project’s design space, so that I could use some of their elements more or
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
less directly. 
 
 
41 
==PAGE:43==
​3​ Analysis 
 
The aim of this chapter is to answer my second research question: “​How can the rhetorical elements
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that are important in Integrated Design-Build co-design be found out?​” 
But why do I need to find them in the first place? Because in order to optimally chart out
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the solution space, one needs a sound foundation of data about the problem and audience spaces:  
“​The whole point of doing research is to extract reliable knowledge from either the natural or
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
artificial world, and to make that knowledge available to others in re-usable form​”. (Cross, 1999).  
As such, in this chapter I elucidate my analytic process for finding a set of topoi that I will later
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
synthesize into a more or less universal, re-usable form. This chapter concludes with a discussion
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
of the methodology’s weaknesses, a model of my cognitive process flow during the analysis, and an
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
overview of a promising automated alternative.  
 
​3.1​ Methodology 
​3.1.1​ Cases 
 
The core of the research was the recording and analysis of audio from two Integrated Design-Build
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
Big Room design workshops, where ideas and issues were recorded on floor plans with post-its and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sketches. The projects took place in Finland, and were conducted in Finnish. Both had the aim of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
begetting a new sociotechnical system next to an existing one before smashing the old one down . 
9
●
Hospital​: Two half-hour sessions of refining spatial functions with representatives of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
future building’s users (mainly nurses and doctors), around draft plans of relevant wards.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The plans were based on previous sessions with the same people; this was the second
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
iteration of the wards. Three architects each embedded him/herself into a group of two to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
six people, asking and answering questions, but mainly listening to the discussion. The first
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
9 The hospital is located in Hämeenlinna, and the two schools (of which only one was the focus of the session that I
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
observed) in Vantaa’s Rajatorppa and Hämeenkylä respectively. I advise the reader to visit the results of these projects
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
to witness what cutting-edge educational and healthcare architecture is/was like in the early 21th century (though the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospital will likely be rebuilt again after three decades, according to one of its architects, so it may be too late for you.)  
 
42 
==PAGE:44==
session was with representatives from the maternity/children’s ward, the second with those
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of the psychiatric ward.  
●
School​: Two different, simultaneous meetings, lasting half an hour. Afterwards the groups,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of five people each, switched with each other, and an equivalent session was held. The topic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of one meeting was the refinement of the spatial configuration and functions. That of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
other was the building’s location on the lot. The groups were composed of architects, design
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
managers, engineers, users, and a client representative (a member of the city council). Here
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
too the majority of the design was already complete; the requirement and wish elicitation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from the school’s children and staff had been performed a couple months before. 
 
In the school, the two groups that ideated simultaneously did so in different rooms. However, in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
hospital, the groups not only worked in the same space, but there were three or four of them in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
each session. As my recording capacity was limited to two devices, I therefore missed a fair amount
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
 
 
of data. 
 
​3.1.2​ Topos extraction and curation 
 
I used ATLAS.ti to transcribe the recordings, yielding a set of topoi. The process was as follows: 
1.
Delineated the transcription into units, each dealing with (roughly) one theme.  
2.
Read through the transcriptions over and over again, finding recurring topoi. Naturally this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
step was not truly separate from the transcription and the above step. 
3.
Coded each unit with the topoi occurring in it.  
 
Each unit was usually a few utterances long; some contained only one or two utterances due to the
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
impossibility of deciphering the utterances surrounding them thanks to the low recording quality,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and many likewise were riddled with missing words and sentences. Note that due to the nature of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
such free creative discussion, the theme of each unit, and therefore where the units interfaced, was
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
often unclear. The topoi are generic, while the themes were usually particular to these kinds of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
projects. 
I began collecting my set of topoi by considering Hermogenes’ six circumstance topoi in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
light of their universal nature. However, I eschewed ​Place​, ​Person and ​Way​, as they are always latent
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in the Task model of architectural design, and adopted the other three. Note that those would by no
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
means be latent in the Process and Team models (e.g. ​Person​: ‘Who is going to make the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
renderings?’; ​Way​: ‘How are we going to achieve that in only two weeks?’) The reasons for my
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considering only the Task model are the following: 
43 
==PAGE:45==
1.
In workshops, the project’s overall Process model is usually effectively formed in separate
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
subsessions devoted to it. This was true in the cases that I observed. 
2.
The Process and Team models have no relevance for fulfilling the intervention’s second
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
outcome (thorough ideation), stated in Section 1.3.1. 
3.
Taking the Process and Team into account would considerably raise the intervention’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complexity, and thus could be inimical to the design game criteria in Section 2.5.2. 
 
I analyzed the data, ending up with a total of 32 topoi using the procedure outlined in Figure 10. It is
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
     
a series of five primary requirements and two contingency requirements that each topos candidate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
has to fulfill; ‘Y’ means that the requirement is satisfied, ‘N’ means it is not. They are formulated so
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
as to ensure that the topoi are maximally relevant to any Integrated Design-Build project and,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considering the diverse target users of my intervention, universally understandable.  
 
 
Figure 10: The topos curation process. 
 
44 
==PAGE:46==
Looking beyond this analysis to the designing of the solution in Chapter 4, the requirement of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
general understanding inherent in the fourth step in Figure 10 (​Not too technical; explorable by anyone​)
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
is the foremost element up to now that contributes towards mental model alignment: it diminishes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
the need for group members who lack technical expertise to form futile mental models of things
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that they will not be able to properly explore, while forcing the group’s technical experts to simplify
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
their language to the lowest common denominator. In other words, that step is for reducing the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
issue that “​The indeterminacy of translations across object worlds is rather severe; one participant’s rabbit
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
parts may be another’s cantilever beam​.​” (Bucciarelli, 2002) 
 
​3.1.3​ Coding  
 
In the following examples of coding, there are instances of different people’s utterances having the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
same topos within a single unit. These are shown only to illuminate to the reader the mental
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
process of topos discernment; in the actual finding of co-occurrences, multiples were folded
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
together into a single code, as the vast majority of multiples were in fact of the exact same specific
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
topic, e.g. the first two instances of ​Act / process in Unit X+1 of the hospital case are both
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
fundamentally about holding a meeting. Incidentally, note also that some utterances that have the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topos ​Act / process​, like the second utterance in the hospital case Unit X (about mirroring), I did not
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
code because they refer to the designers themselves instead of the project’s envisioned users.
