​3.2​ Results 
 
The set of topoi is displayed in Figure 11. The lines show my understanding of the topoi’s conceptual
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
hierarchy, where every topos sources from Hermogenes’ six circumstance topoi, shown as larger
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
circles. Note that ​Place​, ​Way and ​Person are shown only for completeness: as explained above, I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
omitted them due to their overt ubiquity in an architectural context rendering them rather futile. 
 
 
 
Figure 11: The 32 topoi that resulted from the analysis. The larger circles are Hermogenes’ ​circumstance topoi,​ of which 
the grey ones I omitted due to their overt ubiquity in an architectural context rendering them rather futile. The lines 
show my intuitive understanding of the topoi’s conceptual hierarchy. Note that ​Nexus hell​ was later renamed to 
Congestion​ to help people whose linguistic aptitude is lacking. 
 
 
48 
==PAGE:50==
Having coded the units, I used ATLAS.ti to find the topoi’s co-occurrences, i.e. how many times they
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
occurred with each other in the same unit. Here I call the topoi that occur with each other ​affiliates​.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
For example, one topos with a relatively high amount of co-occurrences (23) was ​Play​, resulting
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from the nature of the two cases: there was much discussion of recreational activities for the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
school, and one of the hospital sessions was about the children’s ward. The most common affiliates
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of ​Play (3 co-occurrences each) were ​Rest​, ​Sharing and ​Separate / unified​; the first testifies to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
tendency of people to play during breaks in their work or studying and thus to situate playful
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
activities in spaces reserved for rest, while the other two originate from discussions of whether to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
integrate children’s playing areas in the hospital with adults’ rest areas and whether to share the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
school’s sports hall with external actors. Moreover, an example from the low end is ​Extendability​,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
with only two affiliates: ​Future and ​Cause​. The discussion was about whether to take into account
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the possibility of the pupils of a nearby tiny school being transferred to the school under
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
envisionment by leaving space for an extension on the building’s lot. (Note that despite the poor
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
evidence for the relevance of the ​Extendability topos, I retained it according to the first contingency
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
requirement shown in Figure 10: though it was rare in these cases, my mental model of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
architectural projects tells me that it has great relevance in many others.) 
Figure 12 shows a sampling of the co-occurrences, for seven topoi. The numbers at the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
left-hand side are the total co-occurrences for the respective topoi. Note that most co-occurrences
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(for example the two of ​Extendability​) are of size one, so that they are statistically negligible. And
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
even the larger ones, such as the four occurrences between ​Central / peripheral and ​Visual connection​,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
may well be just an anomaly of these two cases. 
 
49 
==PAGE:51==
 
Figure 12: The co-occurrences of a selection of topoi. The numbers at the left-hand side are the total co-occurrences for 
the respective topoi. 
 
 
 
Examples of the categorization of utterances under certain topoi are listed in Appendix D. 
 
 
 
 
50 
==PAGE:52==
​3.3​ Discussion 
 
In this section I will first describe the weaknesses present in the above methodology. Then I give an
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
overview of the mental process underlying the methodology, in order for the reader to gain a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
deeper understanding between rhetoric and mental models in design practice. I conclude with an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
outline of another topos curation process that I tried. 
 
​3.3.1​ Heurism 
 
While design is almost always heuristic, the methodology described above was remarkably riddled
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with uncertainty and fuzziness: 
●
Small dataset.  
○
Only two cases, giving a total recording time of only around five hours. 
○
Due to the low recording quality of the devices (iPhone 4 and MacBook Pro), and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
their poor positioning, as well as multiple groups talking at the same time in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hospital case, much of the recordings were undecipherable. 
●
Inadequacy of audio. The sessions were mediated by 2D draft plans of the buildings, so that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
the participants conveyed much of their thinking by pointing at them; this data is lacking in
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
the audio recordings. 
●
Subjective topos curation. Looking at Figure 10, it is clear that all of the steps except “​Not
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
describing a specific spatial function​” are more or less subjective. Even the fulfillment of the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirement for commonness is sometimes subject to interpretation, as the topoi are not
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
always explicit, but appear latently and have to be ‘read between the lines’. 
●
Subjective coding. Again, due to the non-explicit nature of much of the data, many
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instances of code assignment were subject to my interpretation. ​Cause can be interpreted in
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
a particularly wide range of ways. 
●
Ambiguous unit delineation. It was in some cases not clear where one unit should end and
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the next one begin, affecting the co-occurrence results. 
 
There is therefore no denying that this whole work can be considered not only a study in rhetoric
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
and design, but also in ambiguity. For despite the remarkable heurism, the solution (described in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the next chapter) did not turn out awry. Indeed, when comparing rates of completion and therefore
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
rates of iterative improvement, ​good enough​ is far superior to ​perfect​.  
 
51 
==PAGE:53==
​3.3.2​ Mental model process flow 
 
My analytic process for coding the dataset with topoi is summarized in Figure 13. By scrutinizing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
the data through the lens of my existing mental model of architecture and human nature, my mind
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
enriched the mental model of these particular cases (the school and the hospital) that I already had
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
from visiting the sessions. This little case-specific mental model then grew all the way through
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
until the end of the dataset. At some point near the beginning of this growth, it and the general
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
mental model together enabled me to recognize some concept in some utterance as equivalent to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some other ones handled previously, and so I formalized that concept as a topos, initializing my
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
topos mental model. In such a way the set of topoi bloomed. Having then gone through the dataset
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and ended up with the 32 topoi, I scanned the dataset once again, coding it with the topoi. 
Note that Figure 13 is only a simplification, for I did not go through the dataset totally
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
linearly (going back now and then, gaining new insights), and because mental models tend to rarely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
grow discretely like the figure suggests, instead gradually maturing utterance by utterance. Even
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the topos mental model did not actually grow in a definite fashion, for the topoi’s nuances kept on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interplaying and refining. And sometimes I scrapped topoi, sometimes folded them together. They
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
were somewhat like quantum particles whose wavefunctions gradually condensed.  
 
 
Figure 13: A simplified mental model process flow of my analysis of the data. 
52 
==PAGE:54==
​3.3.3​ More advanced methodology 
 
 
I also tried to find the topoi by exploiting natural language processing. Topic models are statistical
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
models used in text analysis to discover latent topics, using word co-occurrence. So I cleaned the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
dataset, removing insubstantial words such as ‘good’ and ‘this’, as well as overly generic words like
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
‘space’ and ‘area’ that have no value in this context. Then I fed the data into the LDA algorithm,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
commonly used in topic modeling. The specific implementation I used was Termite, a visual tool for
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
assessing the quality of topic models (Chuang et al., 2012) 
The results were somewhat disappointing, with poor statistical coherence for the topics,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and even the most coherent ones offering no new insights. Examples of these ‘topics’: 
●
Hospital 
○
Topic 1: ward, toilet, multifunction, storage, big, door, meeting, customer. 
○
Topic 2: child, corridor, outpatient, workspace, open, rest. 
●
School  
○
Topic 1: lot, noise, hall. 
○
Topic 2: side, supply, big, airplane, storey. 
 
The reason for such futility is clear: the dataset is far too small. “​Such visualizations are challenging to
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
create because of the high dimensionality of the fitted model – LDA is typically applied to many thousands of
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
   
 
   
documents, which are modeled as mixtures of dozens (or hundreds) of topics, which themselves are modeled as
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
distributions over thousands of terms.​” (Sievert & Shirley, 2014). 
In light of this truth, two cases, each consisting of 2-3 hours of recordings, seems laughable;
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
their yield can be considered as an almost random spattering of topics, heavily reflecting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
specifics of the projects and the participants’ characters. Much more data is needed to cover further
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
(possibly numerous) topoi and co-occurrences that these two happened not to cover, in addition to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
drowning out rare ones that these may have been biased towards.  
   
 
 
 
53 
==PAGE:55==
​4​ Synthesis 
 
Now that I have raked together a sound foundation of rhetorical data from actual Integrated
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design-Build workshops, it is finally time to design the solution, while answering my third research
 
     
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
question: “​What kind of intervention would be most efficient in achieving the expected outcomes?​”  
The solution’s format shall be a design game​. This decision sources from the knowledge that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
they manifest a host of values highly relevant to the aims of this project: intuitiveness, an engaging
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
quality, idea generation, and so on. Their mental model alignment potential is also notable, being
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
excellent “​in creating a play spirit where a shared language is actively being constructed through design game
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
materials.​” (Vaajakallio, 2012) 
My design process will loosely follow the ​plan-act-observe-reflect structure of action research:
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
each section headed '​N​th version' will comprise the ​reflection and ​planning steps, while those headed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
'​N​th test' will detail the subsequent ​acting and ​observing steps. The reason for the looseness of my
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
approach is the intense flux between the problem and solution spaces, as well as their immensity,
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that I experienced during the first two of the four iterations, so that it was simply far easier to mix
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
the steps. This is also the ground for my abstaining from the more rigorous alternative structure of
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
requirements – solution alternatives – evaluation – selection – testing – refinement​; while each testing
   
 
   
   
   
   
 
 
 
 
section does include a list of requirements for the next version, the sheer amount of details to be
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
decided and their impact on each other would have made their explicit evaluation and selection
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
impractical. 
Having been convinced of the efficacy of Halstrøm's approach, there is no reason not to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
begin my design process with it. I will therefore do so, beginning with the values to be celebrated: 
1.
Thorough probing of the design space early on. 
2.
The formation of a shared mental model. 
3.
Design game values. 
 
Note that the first two are the solution’s overall objectives, given in Section 1.3.1, while the last one
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
is an auxiliary value, intended to reinforce the others. Figure 14 shows a preliminary attempt at
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
amplifying these values by a mixture of processual (what I should do) and objective (what the
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution should be like and what the audience i.e. the players should do) ideas. 
 
54 
==PAGE:56==
 
Figure 14: Values that my solution ought to celebrate are shown in grey, while proposals to amplify them are shown in 
green, with associated topoi from Hermogenes. 
 
Having already provided an overview of the optimal ​way to design design games in Section 2.5, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
having there also absorbed high-potential details which to imbue my solution space with from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
examples of design games with similar aims to this one, I will without further ado proceed onto the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
actual designing. 
 
 
​4.1​ Design process 
 
Here I explain in detail the reasoning behind my design decisions through the four versions on my 
solution, as well as describing the tests between each. 
 
​4.1.1​ First version 
 
Physicality boosts thinking, as the body acts as a cognitive extension of the brain, as encapsulated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
in Tversky’s embodied cognition (Tversky & Hard, 2009) and Watsuji’s embodied intersubjectivity
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
55 
==PAGE:57==
(Krueger, 2013). In addition, as implied in these two sources, physicality prompts people to take
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
others’ perspectives, inducing empathy and mental model alignment. And being familiar with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Vaajakallio’s principles of the benefits of physical boundary objects (Section 2.5.4) (and not being a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
digital engineer), I would not exploit the fruits of this age by making a digital solution. It should
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
rather be a tangible one. And the first proposal I made to myself was that it would be a card-based
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
one. The reasoning was simple: a deck of cards is an excellent medium for distributing to many
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
people multifaceted data in a standardized format, and is hence naturally suited for embodying
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
topoi. The most obvious design would feature one topos per card alongside a visual depiction of it,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
and possibly some auxiliary information.  
Having established this primordial physical format, I was unable to gradually build up the
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rules in a systematic fashion to arrive at a plausible solution: a game requires a definite vision in
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
order to have a chance of becoming truly fun and useful. Besides, the solution space is huge, with a
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
myriad vastly different formats that could be implemented. I therefore needed to let my mind dwell
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
deeply in the solution, problem and audience spaces in order to create a rich mixture of concepts,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
conducive for abduction. Eventually I did abduce an idea that seemed brimming with potential:
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
since I had developed my analytic process with inspiration from Halstrøm’s rhetorical framework,
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and the cornerstone of this solution was thus already composed of the concept of the topoi therein,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
why not base my solution fully on it? After all, Value 1. above is inherent in Halstrøm’s framework,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
 
 
while there is a powerful (though not yet fully charted) connection between rhetoric and mental
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
models (Value 2.), and the framework’s iterative nature seems to render it well translatable into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
game mechanics (Value 3.). So I decided to frame the game as a manifestation of Halstrøm’s
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
three-step process in the context of Integrated Design-Build. First the players would choose values
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to celebrate in their project; next they would amplify the values by coming up with ideas inspired
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
by the topoi; lastly they envision the impact of those ideas on the project’s users and on themselves. 
The key question now was the mechanics that would be used to amplify a value by a topos
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
card. It is obvious that a lazy yet quite effective approach would be to use the cards as trivial
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sources of inspiration for traditional brainstorming, by the group randomly choosing one or a few
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
of them at each step and simply discussing what comes to their minds, in relation to some value to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
   
celebrate. Such a tool could miss Value 3 above, would probably have scarcely any mental model
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alignment effect, and though going some way towards fulfilling Value 1, would ultimately fail to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
exploit the relationships between the topoi. Now, I deemed the matrix of affiliations between the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topoi to be sufficient, at least for this first version, as the data which would drive the game. So how
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to make the players consider affiliated topoi together? In the case of a digital game, the system
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
could simply offer the players all the cards that are affiliated with the one they previously chose.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
But in this physical case, the players would have to find out those connections by themselves.  
The most obvious way to achieve this is to include the names of the affiliate topoi on each
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
card. However, given two topoi under consideration, could not the relevance of a third one be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
56 
==PAGE:58==
increased, and hence Value 1 further amplified, by picking it not from one or the other of the
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
affiliate sets, but from their intersection? If the sets were of size 1-3, the intersection would be
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rapid enough to find manually, but then there most likely would not be an intersection; at least
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
around six topoi would ensure a high enough probability of finding at least one common affiliate.
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
But having to compare word sets of that size would of course slow down the game and diminish the
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
flow state.  
So lacking the power of computation, from the subset of the solution space comprised of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
medium-specific tricks I had to find a method to physically achieve logical conjunction. Here I
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
again abduced a solution, grounded in the knowledge that paper lends itself to modification (ie.
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
origami and ​kirigami​, the arts of paper folding and paper cutting) that enables information and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
functions to be embedded in its structure and transmittance of light. Just like electrons in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
semiconductors are harnessed to perform agonizing work that human brains once had to endure,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
so can the electrons in paper be harnessed: thanks to impurities and to the atoms’ band structure,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the electrons reflect and absorb photons instead of letting them pass, rendering the material
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
opaque. In other words, by creating a discrete subset of the Cartesian plane with a one-to-one
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
mapping to the total set of topoi, and laying the subset on the cards and cutting a hole at each point
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
whose mapping is in the topos’ affiliate set, then overlaying cards on each other would yield the
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
logical conjunction, visible as the remaining holes in the unity. Placing this stack on a diagram of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
the mapping, the affiliate topoi in question would appear as dots next to their names (Figure 15). 
The affiliate holes I placed as a series along the cards’ edges. However, as cutting the holes
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would require laser cutting, for this first version I substituted the holes with variously colored dots;
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the cards’ edges would be placed next to each other for comparison, after which an external chart
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would be consulted (Figure 16B). 
 
  
Figure 15: The photonic technique for performing logical conjunction on multiple topoi’s sets of affiliates. In this case
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the two colored dots reveals the intersection to be ​In-out interface​ and ​Nexus hell​.  
 
The next issue was naturally the cards’ shape, which would depend on / affect their spatial
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
connection method, if any. I ended up with a hexagon (Figure 16A), not only because it is more
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
unique than a rectangle, but also as it possesses both the universal linkability of a square and a bit
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
57 
==PAGE:59==
of the the phenomenological delight of a circle. This decision concretized the design to the extent
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that it became trivial to establish the value amplification mechanism as simply a player adding a
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
card next to one or more cards, while suggesting to the group an idea inspired by that topos, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hopefully also of the adjacent ones, that relates to the value. The players would then hopefully
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discuss the idea deeply enough to fulfill the last stage of Halstrøm’s framework. Moreover, in order
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
to ensure that a shared mental model of the importance of the idea would solidify, the group
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
assigns one of the following three levels to it: ​Must have​,​ Wonderful to have​ or​ Nice to have​. 
The last aspect to consider was the game’s goal for the players. I created a playing field
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
(Figure 16C) with four hexagons around a value card, separated from it by two or three topos cards,
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
so that the goal would be to reach those hexagons from the value card. Thus the players are forced
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to make at least a decent amount of ideas, while having a sense of purpose. This amplifies the
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
solution’s Value 1, namely bountiful ideation, while emphasizing its game-like quality, Value 3. 
 
 
Figure 16: The materials of the first version. The text, which may not be readable, is simply the names of the topoi. ​A​. A 
selection of the topos cards; ​B​.  The mapping between the colored dots on the cards and the topoi; ​C​. The playing field. 
 
 
Summary of the rules 
 
1.
Brainstorm the project’s values, writing them down on blank hexagonal cards. 
2.
Place a value card on the playing field’s central hexagon. 
3.
Someone is inspired by a topos card for how to amplify the value, puts the card next 
to it, and explains the idea.  
4.
The group discusses the idea, and they assign an importance to it. 
5.
Everyone in turn chooses a place to put the next card. 
6.
If the place is next to existing topos cards, the common affiliate cards between them 
are taken out (at most four). 
58 
==PAGE:60==
7.
Return to step 3, unless the surrounding four goal hexagons have been reached, in 
which case the same is repeated with another value. 
 
Note that there are countless more sophisticated possibilities for the rules, particularly due to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hexagon’s universal linkability, and the vagueness of what is best in inducing creativity. However, I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
tried to follow a principle of simplicity rooted in design game theory, in the knowledge that
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complexity is likely to hinder the formation of the play spirit, and diminish the flow state.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Moreover, I knew that like a product, the core aspects of the solution ought to be tested before
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
complexity is added, and I infer that like a product, it is valid to do so here. 
 
​4.1.2​ First test 
 
 
I evaluated the first version with four participants: two staff and two PhD students in DTU’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Management Engineering department (Figure 17). Although such a narrow range of professional
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
backgrounds is far from optimal given this project’s purpose, it did not matter for the first test,
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which yielded mainly general aspects for improvement that anyone could have pointed out. In fact,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the participants’ design game expertise enabled them to provide highly profitable suggestions. As
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
such, my sampling was ​purposive​, as it was based on “​the researcher's judgement as to typicality or
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
interest​” (Robson 1993, p.142). Though it was far from perfect, in Robson’s words “​[t]he exigencies of
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
carrying out real world studies can mean that the requirements for representative sampling are very difficult,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
if not impossible, to fulfill.​”   
In order for the group to simulate not only design managers and architects, but also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
representatives of the future users, the group’s task was tailored to fit its members’ collegiate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experience. Namely, they used the prototype to probe the solution and problem space for an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
imaginary case of a new college dorm complex to be built on DTU’s campus.  
Though the test’s aims were to compare the mental model alignment and ideation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
effectiveness of the design game to those of traditional brainstorming by interviews and analysis of
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
the generated concepts, the test uncovered such grievous shortcomings (mostly related to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
playing experience) that any comparison to a baseline was rendered pointless. In other words, this
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
test was akin to hewing, while later ones were like chiseling and sandpapering.  
The weaknesses were the following: 
●
It was slightly difficult for the players to come up with solid values for the project,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
due to the suddenness of the task. After all, they had gained only a minimal mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
model of their simulated project, and only a few minutes ago. As such, their mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
59 
==PAGE:61==
models of the project’s most important values were inadequate upon starting the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
amplification, and only began to solidify during it. 
●
The players had to be reminded multiple times of the need to assign each idea an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
importance after discussing about it. They also forgot about the color rule, trying to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
place a topos card next to other ones without checking whether it was allowed. 
●
After the first idea, the players forgot that their ideas should amplify the value that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was written on the card in the middle of the board, so the ideation veered off course. 
●
They found the color rule too restrictive, preferring instead to get inspired freely by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
any topos. And they deemed the game’s whole spatial structure jarring for their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
creativity. 
●
When it came time to assign an importance to each idea, the players had a hard time
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
remembering all of the ideas they had come up with during their discussion. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
game should thus include continuous documentation of their concepts. 
●
The cards were much too small. 
 
In addition, an issue that came up regarding the testing itself was that as the players’ project was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
imaginary, they lacked the full motivation to consider their ideas in light of the seriousness of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
task. In other words, according to them, their lack of responsibility affected their ideation. 
 
 
Figure 17: Players trying the game (and a design expert observing in the background to provide additional feedback).  
60 
==PAGE:62==
​4.1.3​ Second version 
 
The dominant impression that the participants made on me was of the need to free up the players’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
creativities, perhaps by letting them brainstorm individually too, without the suffocating feeling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that constant discussion (for introverts at least) and rules induced in them. Hence I established the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
integration of individual ideation as the cornerstone of this version.  
The test participants’ distaste of the strict nature of the rules for placing the cards
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
presented a critical dilemma: if I were to remove it, this solution would lose its connection with the
   
 
     
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
empirical data I accumulated in the Analysis phase, making it just a trivial brainstorming tool, a
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
nice set of inspirational architectural keywords. So it was clear that I had to arrive at some kind of
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
   
 
   
balance between the current state and that naïve vision. I did this by throwing the whole
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
hexagon-based system into the bin, while preserving and enriching a simpler form of the affiliated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
topos system. This time the players would not have to find common affiliates between multiple
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topoi: those of one at a time would have to suffice. As such, there was no need for the logical
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conjunction mechanism (which I was proud of), and the affiliates could be written directly on the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topos cards (Figure 18), which are folded as accordions, with only the top topos visible. Note that I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
limited the number of affiliates to five in order to not overwhelm the players; more mental primes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
would probably hinder creativity.  
The amplification phase would now include an individual part: each player simply takes a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
card that inspires him/her with a concept (anywhere in the design space), writes it down, then
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
opens the card and by viewing the affiliated topoi therein, tries to either further heighten the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept, or create a totally new one. Afterwards he/she replaces the card and takes another one,
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and this continues until a time limit has been reached. They write each idea on an blank card and
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
add it next to the value card, or next to an existing card if the new idea is a continuation of a
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
     
 
   
previous one, each player hence forming one or more concept clusters. Adding the ideas into clear
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
clusters next to a large value card, the players will probably remember their aim better, perhaps
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
solving the third issue in the list of issues above. In addition, the act of writing not only aids the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
formation the writer’s mental model and helps the group to refer to the ideas later, but also eases
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the analysis of the ideas afterwards by the architects. This concept of leaving visual traces of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design process is discussed comprehensively by Vaajakallio (2012, p. 179). 
It should also be noted that I added an icon for each topos in order to provide a visual
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
creative boost. I tried to make some icons non-intuitive, helping the players to break outside
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
normal patterns of thought. Such icons thus nudge the user to interpret the topos in multiple ways;
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
in effect, they are topoi that enrich the actual topoi, and could be called ​‘metatopoi’​. For example,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instead of a plant for ​Nature​, a whale; instead of a car for ​Logistics​, a galleon; instead of a robot for
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
61 
==PAGE:63==
Future​, a vinyl (an artefact of a past future, yet one that is again relevant for lovers of high-quality
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
music). 
Now, how to integrate the ideas generated by this method between all the minds? The most
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
obvious method is making the players freely discuss about them. Thus each person shall describe
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
his/her cluster in turn, after which the group deliberates on it, perhaps offering further ideas into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the mix. However, it may be that this is not an adequate method of achieving the mental model
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alignment, as depending on the players’ mindsets and experience, the deliberation could stay on an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
abstract level, in which case a mind tends not to easily absorb new information. So I decided to
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
append the common mental model solidification technique of storytelling as the final step: the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
players create a brief narrative of an envisioned user experiencing some or all of the elements in
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the cluster. To ensure an adequate play spirit, they will have some boundary objects; a few paper
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
artefacts representing the users. Moreover, at first I considered making one of the players write
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
down the story, but eventually eschewed such a scheme due to the time it would take to write it
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
down, and its limited benefit: only the scribe would gain a boost in his/her memory of the notions
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
that are written down. 
 
 
Figure 18: A selection of the new topos cards. Each one is folded, with only the top topos visible until a player opens it. 
 
Next, in order to fix the inefficiency of the value brainstorming phase in forming a shared mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
model of the project’s values, I expanded it by integrating an individual step into it too, and adding
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
inspirational cards. Namely, I thought about how to harness metaphors, which are a foundational
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
aspect of creativity and the formation of shared understanding, as elaborated in Section 2.4. Each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
card corresponds to a metaphor that could be used as a lens for considering the players’ actual
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
project. Similarly to the amplification phase, each player views the cards, and upon inspiration
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from one of them, opens it to reveal three inspirational pictures (Figure 19). But unlike with the
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topoi, my curation of the metaphors and their associated pictures is completely subjective, as I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
deemed an intuitively wide enough range of verbal and visual concepts related to architecture to be
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
sufficient for a prototype. Furthermore, like with the topos icons, I tried to choose some pictures so
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
as to jolt the players’ patterns of thought away from their well-worn (sometimes even clichèd)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tracks, fostering consideration of the words’ non-standard associations.
Indeed, “​successful
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
metaphors rely on the unlikeness of things, not upon their likeness. The characteristic of an effective metaphor
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
62 
==PAGE:64==
is it borrows an image from one schema of ideas, and applies it to another, previously unrelated schema.​”
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
(Forty, 2000)  
 
 
Figure 19: A selection of the contents of the metaphor cards, each one having three inspirational images.  
Top left: Island. Top right: Church. Bottom left: Navy. Bottom right: Farm. 
 
Having chosen such a card and seen the pictures, the player writes on blank cards as many
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
values that he/she can think of which explain why that metaphor suits his/her vision of how the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project’s result should turn out. Now once again, the individual ideas (in this case, values) ought to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
be synthesized into a shared mental model. In order to conserve and heighten the players’ flow
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
state, I decided to keep the rules trivial: each player in turn describes one of his/her values, after
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which the group decides whether to place it alone as the seed of a cluster, or place it into an
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
existing cluster. When every value has been assigned so, the group names the clusters (the name
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could be the same as one of the values therein), writing it onto a large (emphasizing the value’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
importance) circular (emphasizing the holistic nature of this process) card. This process is in effect
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
equivalent to the Framing step of Dorst’s ​Frame creation​ (Dorst et al., 2016). 
Now with such similar boundary objects and structure in the two phases, the game seems 
more balanced, and its purpose clearer to players.  
 
 
 
 
 
63 
==PAGE:65==
Summary of the rules 
 
Values 
1.
Individual 
1.1.
Each player views the metaphor cards, and takes one whose metaphor suits his/her
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
vision of the project. 
1.2.
The player views the inspirational pictures therein, and writes down all the values
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
present in the metaphor that he/she can come up with, on the blank cards. 
1.3.
The player replaces the metaphor card and repeats the above steps until 5 min has
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
passed.  
2.
Group 
2.1.
Each player presents the metaphors that he/she used, briefly describing how it fits
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
his/her vision of the project. 
2.2.
Everyone lays down one of his/her value value cards in turn, organically clustering
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them (optimally 3-5 clusters would emerge). 
2.3.
They integrate each cluster into a single concept and write it on a large circular
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
card. These concepts are the overall values of the project. 
Amplification 
Each value is amplified in turn. The circular card is placed in the middle of the table. 
1.
Individual 
1.1.
Each player views the topos cards, and takes one that inspires some idea relating to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
the value. The idea could belong to the problem or solution spaces, or both. 
1.2.
The player writes down the concept in a blank idea card. 
1.3.
He/she opens the topos card and views the affiliated topoi, whereby he/she is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inspired with further ideas and writes them on blank idea cards. Each one could
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
either be an amplification of the original idea, or a totally new one (that amplifies
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
the value).  
1.4.
The player places the idea cards on the rim of the circular value card. 
2.
Group 
2.1.
Each player presents his/her ideas, and the group briefly discusses them, perhaps
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
coming up with some further amplifications for it. 
2.2.
The group creates a story where an envisioned user experiences some or all of these
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
ideas.  
 
64 
==PAGE:66==
​4.1.4​ Second test 
 
 
I evaluated the second version with three students from DTU: two civil engineers and one
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
management engineer (Figure 20). Therefore in this case the participants represented more closely
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the configuration of professional backgrounds in Integrated Design-Build projects. The task was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the same as in the previous test: ideating for a new college dorm. This time, due to the increased
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
amount of steps in the game, instead of detailing the whole gameplay at the start, I only outlined
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
the structure, and then explained the mechanics of each step just before the players began that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
step. I also gave examples of each step, for an imaginary school project. Note that once again, there
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was time to amplify only one value. 
 
 
Figure 20: The second test’s participants performing the amplification. 
 
The following table details the questions and answers of this test:

rules?

and observation.

should come up with using the topos cards. 
Is the format 
conducive of the 
flow state? 
Observation.

Value-finding

enough, with as much as 27 values, grouped into

clusters,
and

clustering was lively. However, the Amplification
 
 
 
 
 
 
was disappointing: the creation of ideas did not
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
seem easy for two of the participants (one came
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
up with only two). And the last step was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
remarkably awkward, with lengthy thinking
 
 
 
 
 
pauses in the discussion, barely any amplification
   
 
 
 
 
 
of others’ ideas, and each story being told
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
primarily by one person only.

amplification from 
a multitude of 
viewpoints? 
Observation of the 
created ideas. 
They amplified their value, ‘Learning and sharing

1.

This version’s weaknesses were thus the following:  
●
The players did not add ideas enough to each other’s concepts in the last step. 
●
One player found it hard to ideate, even with the topos cards, perhaps due to the classical
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
problem of self-censoring. 
●
They were quite fixated on one concept (a library), adding details to it, at the cost of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
omitting the rest of the vast design space.  
●
Once again, the players sometimes forgot that their ideas should amplify the value (though
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
not as much as last time). 
●
It was difficult for them to create stories of even mediocre quality, and to collaborate in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
creating them.
