66 
==PAGE:68==
4.1.5​ Third version 
 
 
The foremost modification I made for this version was based on the fourth point in the list above,
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as well as my desire to give the game a whopping boost in play spirit and aesthetic quality by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
hauling it into the third dimension. How could the players be nudged to always keep in mind their
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
task of amplifying the specific value that is written on a card in the middle of the table? By an
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
embodied metaphor (Section 2.4): making them somehow physically connect the ideas into the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
value in a way that has the power to conjure up a variety of mental associations. So I thought of the
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
   
   
 
act of pushing a stick into a hole, with the ideas attached to the stick’s other end. If so, the whole
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
structure could resemble a flower on a stalk. There is scarcely a more visually pleasing metaphor
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
for amplification than a flower blooming. And creating colorful, pliable flowers is a far more
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
engaging experience than arranging cards on a table. For example, it is hard to imagine anyone
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
except the most apathetic person being emotionally untouched by the sight of a bloom with a lone
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
petal; we want to ‘cure’ it by adding more. 
Thus the cards on which the players write their ideas should be shaped like petals, and be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
colored. The ‘amplification base’ in which the stalks are placed I designed as circular (though
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
practical problems pressured me to leave it as a square in the prototype), with the holes in a series
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
along its edge, their amount being such that four players could each add at least three flowers. The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
stalks ought to be bendable, so that it would be easy to see what is written on the petals from a
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
sitting position, and so that the activity would be even more dynamic. Each value would have its
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
own little ‘value base’ (Figure 23B) that is placed into the amplification base. Now, this approach
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could also significantly increase play spirit during the clustering of values in the game’s previous
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phase: instead of arranging the value cards in a random mess, they would be clustered by setting
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them radially on the base, before a circular card is laid on them when integrating the values.  
I initially considered having a single amplification base for each value, without the separate
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
little value bases, but realized that as there would need to be at least five such bases (or more, for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the test yielded as many as six clusters), this solution would result in excessive volume and mass for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
the game; the vision of future facilitators awkwardly lugging the game around made me reconsider.
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
As such, the flowers have to be taken off the base before each new value is amplified. 
It is worth noting that another benefit of the above boosts to the materiality of the game is
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
that is eases any possible process of scanning through the produced concepts after the session; for
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
example, an architect may have become fascinated by some cluster of ideas, and instead of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
referring to a recording of the session (if any), can now simply look at the specific bloom of ideas,
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
which is preserved by the petals-stem structure, unlike a cluster of cards on a table. This is an
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
example of leaving visual traces, as expounded by Vaajakallio (2012, p. 179). 
67 
==PAGE:69==
A detail worth deliberating is my decision to have two holes on the petals, instead of one
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
like for the value cards (‘leaves’), so that when they are folded together, the petal curves with two
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
axes of curvature. This can be done in either direction, resulting in a convex or a concave bloom.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
The advantages of this gimmick are the following: 
1.
Without it, the petals would droop, a sight which people naturally associate with poor
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
health or laziness. Not only am I foregoing this negative nudge, but the aesthetic tension of
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
the double curvature is indeed a positive nudge, radiating mental energy.  
2.
It renders the blooms more realistic, achieving a better overall balance between
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concreteness and abstraction, which is critical for supporting creativity (Section 2.5.4). 
3.
Players without much paper folding experience, or indeed handicraft experience in general,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
will hopefully be delighted with the extreme ease of the operation by which they can create
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
a fairly realistic-looking bloom. 
4.
The convex configuration is a cue for openness, arousing associations with the benefits of
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
collectivistic thinking. On the other hand, the concave one could prime the participants (at
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
least the more sensitive ones) with a sense of safety, strengthening their magic circle
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Section 2.5.2) so that they would be less inhibited in the next step (discussion and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
storytelling). In addition, making the ideas written on the petals visible only from a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
relatively small angle from the front, the form could impart a feeling of partial secrecy, a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
sense of preciousness, fostering a feeling of valuable work being performed; “​I am making
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
something worthwhile​”. 
 
Next up was solving the passivity that the players exhibited when discussing; they were disinclined
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to add to each other’s ideas, and in general to deliberate about them. This will probably already be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to some extent alleviated by the amplification’s new form, with the metaphor of flower growth as a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
cue. And as each player has idea cards (petals) of different colors, the more vibrant-minded players
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
may be disposed to further colorify each other’s blooms. Yet this is not enough to ensure a lively
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
discussion, particularly in a group of introverts and people who are not used to ideation. Now,
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
instead of modifying the discussion and storytelling step ​per se​, I modified the previous step,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
changing it from individual to pairwise ideation, which would proceed by dialogue about both the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ideas themselves, and about how to cluster them as blooms; an intimate, dynamic, kinetic
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
collaboration. This decision has the following advantages: 
1.
Creative juices tend to flow better with discussion. 
2.
Motivation from a feeling of being on a shared mission with the partner. 
3.
Less pressure when presenting the ideas thanks to shared authorship. 
4.
It acts a warmup for whole-group discussion. 
(The pairs should then be changed around when amplifying the next value, achieving a maximally
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
wide range of mental model alignment.) 
68 
==PAGE:70==
 
In order to remedy the last issue in the list above, I considered scrapping the storytelling element,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
so that the game would now end with only the deliberation on each pair’s ideas. The negligible value
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that such short-form storytelling gave in the test simply did not seem worth the time it took up. So
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
I learned that if storytelling is to be integrated into a design game, it probably should be given
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
proper focus instead of treating it as an auxiliary activity like in this time-deficient case, or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
altogether made the substance of the game, as described in Section 2.5.5. However, I am willing to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
give the storytelling the benefit of the doubt, as it could possibly be cured by changing its scope
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
from the whole group to pairs, retaining the dynamic of intimate dialogue from the previous step.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Hence I decided to do so, making each pair envision a scenario based on the other’s ideas. After the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ideation they first briefly explain their ideas and the group hopefully discusses them, maybe even
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
adding more; then they rotate the base, craft stories pairwise and present them in the end. In this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
way assigning the players the responsibility to ‘treat well’ each other’s ideas, showcasing how well
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
they have ‘nurtured them’ at the end, they could gain a boost in motivation. 
To address the second issue in the list above, I thought it sufficient for the facilitator to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
explicitly prime the participants. Therefore in the next test I emphasized the game’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
brainstorm-like quality in the presentation that I used to explain the rules of each step, saying that
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
there are no ‘correct’ ideas and that there should be no criticizing. At the amplification I also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
emphasized that the ideas can be anything in the design space: opportunities or issues, statements
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
or questions. 
Halstrøm persuaded me to drop the ​Furniture topos, arguing that the abstract nature of all
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the other topoi fosters creative thinking, whereas the concreteness of ​Furniture jars such conceptual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
burgeoning by throwing their minds into the domain of actual pieces of furniture they have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
experienced. And happily the abstract version of that topos, ​Adaptability​, already exists within the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
set. In addition, I came to the conclusion that I should delete the ​Supplies topos too, as it is obviously
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
already covered by ​Logistics​ and ​Storage​. As such, the set reduced from 32 to 30 topoi. 
Lastly, I enhanced the topos cards by adding an inspirational image on their other sides
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Appendix A). Most of them are photos of nature, hopefully inspiring biomimetic ideas. Also, since
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
natural sights are in another conceptual domain than architecture and technology, the players’
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
creativities are not restricted by seeing any concrete solutions. I furthermore placed the cards into
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
holders to enable easy browsing (Figure 23E); this way none of them is upside-down for any
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participant, and they are not in a mess on the table. 
 
 
 
69 
==PAGE:71==
​4.1.6​ Third test 
 
I evaluated the third version with four professionals from NCC: an architect, an architectural
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
engineer, a construction architect and a construction manager (Figure 21). Hence the group
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
represented even more closely a realistic Integrated Design-Build group makeup than before, with
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
only the users and client missing. The task was the same as in the previous test: ideating for a new
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
college dorm at DTU’s campus. Even though only one of the participants had studied at DTU, it was
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
best to keep the previous design task since everyone had experience from living in some college
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
dorm, and its design space seems richer than that of a new NCC headquarters. This time I tried to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
   
convey the mechanics and reasoning of the game’s steps as clearly as possible using a slideshow,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
incorporating the kinds of priming described above.  
In this case I had no new questions to answer, focusing instead on whether this version has
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
escaped the issues plaguing the previous one, and how to further fine-tune it. Overall the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants, who would be the very people to employ the game in real projects, deemed it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
promising; they thought that, with some tweaking, it could well be useful in actuality. They also
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
produced a healthy amount of ideas, and seemed to be well absorbed in their task most of the time.
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
The pairwise amplification in particular was a heartwarming sight, with the flow state apparently
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
pervading the group as they eagerly ideated and crafted flowers, ending up with 7 blooms with a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
total of 16 ideas. 
It is also worth noting that the architect said that she already tends to intuitively ideate by a
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
methodology similar to use of the metaphor cards. This can be seen as a sign of aptness of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
game’s Value-finding phase in expanding the design space of the other participants, particularly
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the users, who often have negligible design experience. In other words, the cards directly convey to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
all participants some useful mental models that have usually been solely in architects’ possession. 
So did this version solve the weaknesses of the previous? Partially. This time the players
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
always tried to amplify the value under amplification, and their ideas covered a relatively wide area
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
of the solution space, though nothing in the rest of the design space. The storytelling was better,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with pair A telling a decently coherent story that indeed incorporated some of the pair B’s ideas.
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, pair B did not understand that the story should be a character-based narrative, instead
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
recounting pair A’s concepts with their own framing, and adding some (only verbally). It would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
hence have been best to give them an example of a good story. Moreover, in the previous test the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
participants did not add ideas to each others’ when discussing; this time they did no better. In fact,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
there was not even any discussion: each pair just described their ideas and the other listened. This
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
was not a significant issue here, as they did informally add ideas in the stories, but the more
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussion and contribution there is, the better a shared mental model would form. 
 
70 
==PAGE:72==
 
Figure 21. Left: the pairwise amplification being carried out. Right: the results of one pair’s amplification, the value 
having been ‘Sociality / knowledge sharing’. 
 
I also conducted a rudimentary quantification of the increase in mental model alignment by
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
comparing the participants’ concept maps. This technique (also called Constructed Shared Mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Models) has previously been used in the context of design by Badke-Schaub et al. (2007). The players
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
simply sketched out a map of concepts that relate to their vision of the design task, as many as they
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
could within five minutes. By doing this both before and after playing the game, I could compare
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
how the amount of ideas and their sharedness has increased. For the latter, I wrote down a tally of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
   
how many participants’ maps included each concept, then took away 1 from each tally (as lone ideas
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
are not shared) and summed them. Somewhat surprisingly, the game did not increase the amount
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of ideas, the results being 20 and 21 respectively (the error margin was at least ±3 due to the high
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
subjectivity of deciding whether a concept written on the map was equivalent to another one).
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
However, the sharedness increased from 6 to 15. With effectively the same amount of concepts, this
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
more than doubling of sharedness is a clear indicator of the potential of the game in achieving its
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
first objective. However, one datum is statistically worthless, so that a lot more such testing would
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
have to be performed to reach reliable evidence. Another way to solidify the technique would be to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
compare not only the sharedness of concepts between the participants’ concept maps, but also
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(using connecting lines between the concepts) the shared sequences, links, importances, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
clusters, as outlined by Cash et al. (2017, p. 158). 
Lastly, I received three great suggestions from the participants. Firstly, despite their
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
coming up with a lot of value proposals in the game’s first step, they found the metaphor cards
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
somewhat restrictive due to their textual nature (with the three inspirational pictures in each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
visible only when the card is unfolded), implying that they would be more effective if they were
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
visual. Secondly, it was thought that the integrated values that they came up with were perhaps too
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
vague (e.g. “Sociality / knowledge sharing”), so that it would have been better if they had been
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
framed as an operational question (e.g. “How to maximize and integrate sociality and knowledge
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
71 
==PAGE:73==
sharing?”). Thirdly, while the game’s creatively catalytic power was praised, its usefulness in actual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
projects was seen as lacking due to there being no evaluation of the concepts. For in Integrated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design-Build, it is of course not enough to create a shared understanding of what the project’s
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
result should and could be like; the shared mental model ought to include knowledge of how
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
realizable the concepts are, and which ones should be given priority.  
 
Summarizing all the above, the improvement suggestions from this test are the following: 
●
Emphasize that they should not only describe their ideas but discuss them.  
●
Emphasize the form of the story they have to make: how an envisioned user would 
experience some of the ideas in the envisioned building. Then give an example.  
●
Emphasize that they are not limited to the metaphor cards when generating values. 
●
Frame the integrated values as questions. 
●
Make the metaphor cards visual. 
●
Include an evaluation step. 
 
 
 
 
72 
==PAGE:74==
​4.1.7​ Fourth version 
 
The most critical element for improvement was the addition of an evaluation step, as without it the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
game would have very limited usefulness in actual Integrated Design-Build projects, where the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
economical facet of every concept is critical. Due to the game’s runtime already being high, at least
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
fifty minutes, and further value amplifications in real cases (in the tests there was time to amplify
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
only one value) adding more, I decided to keep this new step simple. Hence, instead of evaluating
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
every concept cluster (bloom), the group would decide on the three most important ones, writing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
them down on an evaluation sheet (Figure 22). (Note that a side effect of this is that they will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
integrate each of the three clusters, framing them with names, just like for the values earlier in the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
game, strengthening the shared mental model formation). They would then assign each of the three
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
a relative cost: low, medium or high. With such an ambiguous selection, the players are forced to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
clarify their economic expectations. Lastly the sheet could be inserted on an extra value base by a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
hole (Figure 23F), before the group moves onto the next value to be amplified.  
However, not all values are equal in importance. Therefore, when all of them have been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
amplified, one last prioritization will be carried out: the ​best concept of each value is chosen, taking
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
into account both the importance and the relative cost. 
Secondly, I modified the metaphor cards by removing the words on them, so that instead of
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
a word, the players would see one of the card’s images (Figure 23A), and open it to view the other
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
two. This way they will not be restricted by my interpretation of the images, enabling them to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
suggest a vaster array of associations. 
 
 
Figure 22: The evaluation card. 
 
 
 
73 
==PAGE:75==
​4.2​ Summary 
 
In this chapter I described my iterative design process, where I informally followed the classical
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
plan-act-observe-reflect approach, with the alternating designing and testing phases comprising
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
reflection + planning​, and ​acting + observing​ respectively.  
My first version was remarkably different than the others. Its nature was much more like
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
what one usually associates with the concept of ‘board games’, with a definite goal, and the topos
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
cards being not only for inspiration, but serving as functional playing material. Though this version
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
at first seemed good to me, the test revealed abysmal deficiencies. So I had no option but to discard
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
the version’s elements almost totally, retaining only the topos structure from the Analysis phase,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and the concept of a card-based game.  
In the second version I diversified the social experiences, dividing the game into a
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
Value-finding and an Amplification/deliberation phase, each containing an individual and a group
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
step. Now instead of the players simply ideating and placing cards on a board throughout the game,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
the rules varied with time, as I morphed the experience to be explicitly aligned with the three
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phases of Halstrøm’s model.  
The second test suggested that I was going in the right direction, with the players deeming
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
it fairly effective in ideation, and glimpses of the flow state apparent. There were still glaring
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
inefficiencies to resolve, for example relating to the lack of fruitful deliberation in the last step, and
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
the difficulty of storytelling. The most significant change that I made for the third version was to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
endow the game with the third dimension, framing the playing experience with the metaphor of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
growing flowers. Then the last iteration was mostly about fine-tuning, with some suggestions for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the facilitator for priming the players verbally. However, the fourth version also includes a hitherto
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
unconsidered aspect of Integrated Design-Build sessions, namely the need for evaluation of ideas
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
based on economic considerations. 
 
 
 
74 
==PAGE:76==
​4.2.1​ Game materials 
 
The materials of the final version are shown in Figure 23. In addition, all cards are viewable in the 
Appendices. 
 
 
Figure 23: The game’s materials. ​A​. A selection of the metaphor cards; ​B​. A value base, with value proposal cards added
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(middle) and then an integrated value card (right); ​C​. Two colors of idea cards (i.e. petals), and a stalk onto which they
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
are inserted; ​D​. A bare amplification base (left) and what it looks like during amplification (right) except for the lack of
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
writing; ​E​. The topos cards in holders; ​F​. Evaluation cards (shown without writing) inserted onto an extra value base
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
after evaluating the idea clusters (blooms) resulting from each amplification round. 
75 
==PAGE:77==
​4.2.2​ Game mechanics 
 
The game’s mechanics are visualized in Figure 24, and summarized textually below. Note that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
though I have categorized everything after the pairwise ideation into the ​Deliberation phase in the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
text below, in practice the ​Discussion & storytelling step can contain amplification too, depending on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
how willing the players are to add to each other’s ideas. Thus the ​Amplification and ​Deliberation
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
phases are shown as overlapping in Figure 24. 
 
 
Figure 24: The game’s mechanics, as well as tips for the facilitator for priming the players (Emphasis). Although I have
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
categorized everything after the pairwise ideation into the ​Deliberation phase in the textual version of these mechanics
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
below, in practice the ​Discussion & storytelling step can contain amplification too, depending on how willing the players
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are to add to each other’s ideas. Thus the ​Amplification​ and ​Deliberation​ phases are here shown as overlapping.  
Note that the arrow from one of the ‘price tags’ in the evaluation step indicates that one concept cluster will
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
be chosen as the most important one for each value when all the values (three in this case) have been amplified. 
 
76 
==PAGE:78==
Value-finding 
1.
Individual ideation 
1.1.
Each player views the metaphor cards, and takes one whose metaphor suits his/her
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
vision of the project (Figure 23A). 
1.2.
The player unfolds the card to view the other two inspirational pictures too, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
writes down all the values present in the metaphor that he/she can come up with,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
on the value proposal cards (Figure 23B). 
1.3.
The player replaces the metaphor card and repeats the above steps until the time
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
limit has been reached.  
2.
Clustering 
2.1.
Everyone inserts one of his/her value value cards in turn onto the value bases
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(Figure 23B), organically clustering them (optimally 3-5 clusters would emerge). 
2.2.
They integrate each cluster into a single sentence and write it on a large circular
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
card (Figure 23B). These are the overall values of the project. They should be written
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
as operational questions (‘How can…’ / ‘How to…’) 
Amplification 
Each value is amplified in turn. The value base is inserted into the amplification base. 
1.
Each pair of players views the topos cards (Figure 23E), and takes one that inspires
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
some idea relating to the value. The idea could belong to anywhere within the design
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
space (i.e. the solution, problem or audience spaces). 
2.
The pair writes down the idea on an idea card and attach it on a stalk (Figure 23C)
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
which they insert into the amplification base. 
3.
They open the topos card and view the affiliated topoi, whereby they are inspired
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with further ideas and write them on idea cards. Each one could either be a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
continuation of the original idea, in which case they attach the card onto the same
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stalk, or a new one whereupon they attach it onto a new stalk. (Figure 23D, right) 
4.
The pairs repeat the above steps until the time limit has been reached.  
 
Deliberation 
1.
Discussion & storytelling 
1.1.
Each pair presents their ideas, and the group briefly discusses them, perhaps
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
coming up with some further amplifications for it. 
1.2.
Each pair creates and presents a story based on the other pair’s ideas. It should be a
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
scenario where an envisioned user experiences some or all of these ideas.  
2.
Evaluation 
77 
==PAGE:79==
The group chooses the three most important idea clusters, naming each and writing it down
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
onto an evaluation card. Then they assign each of the three a relative price (low, medium or
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
high) and add it to the card. Lastly they could insert the card onto an extra value base. 
 
Ending 
When all of the values have been amplified, one last prioritization will be carried out: the ​best
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
concept of each value is chosen from the evaluation cards, taking into account both the importance
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and the relative cost. 
 
 
4.2.3 Facilitation tips 
 
Make sure to: 
1.
Give brief examples from an imaginary case for each of these steps: Individual ideation (how
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
to use the metaphor cards), Pairwise ideation (how to use the topos cards), and Storytelling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(a character-based story integrating one or more of the concepts). 
2.
Prime the players according to the tips in the Emphasis row in Figure 24. 
3.
Change the pairs around between each value, achieving a maximally wide range of mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
model alignment. 
 
The rules of thumb durations that I found to be good from the tests are given below. The steps that
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
are marked as having a natural ending are ones that, given a lot of workshop time to account for
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
any exceptionally long durations, there is no need to force them to end. Note that as I lacked time
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
to test the evaluation step, its optimal duration is yet to be found out. 
 
Step 
Duration  Natural ending 
Individual ideation 
5 min 
No 
Clustering 
15 min 
Yes 
Pairwise amplification 
10 min 
No 
Discussion & storytelling 
15 min 
Yes 
Evaluation 
? 
Yes 
78 
==PAGE:80==
​5​ Discussion 
 
For his Design with Intent toolkit, Lockton ran numerous workshops, achieving ten iterations
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
before the publication of his thesis, some of them based on multiple workshops (Lockton, 2013). As
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
befits the shorter ​timeframe and lesser aims of an MSc thesis, I managed to cram in only four
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
iterations. The resulting intervention nonetheless seems to do its job, at least to some extent. I
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
begin this section by reflecting on its current state, outlining the innovations that I have achieved
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
in this work and answering the first three of my research questions before giving suggestions on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
alternate ways of using the game. Then I proceed onto answering my fourth research question,
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussing aspects that should be taken into account in any future version or any intervention with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
similar aims or analytic basis, and envision more or less futuristic methodologies for overhauling
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the ones that I employed. 
 
​5.1​ Reflection 
 
Here I contemplate the results of this work. First is a summary of the innovations that it can be said
   
 
 
   
 
 
     
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
to contribute. Then follows the explicit answering of the first three of my research questions.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Lastly, I provide suggestions for using the solution. 
 
​5.1.1​ Contributions 
 
What does this thesis bring to the table? Below I describe the contributions to both practice /
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
industry, and to academia. 
 
Practical 
I have provided the construction and architectural industries with an intervention for Integrated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design-Build projects, which helps their stakeholders to form a shared mental model of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expectations for the result, i.e. what the envisioned sociotechnical system should and could be like.  
 
Academic 
I have formulated a methodology for analyzing the rhetorical facet of any architectural co-design
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
session in order to gather together a rhetorical dataset that can be used to design interventions for
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
79 
==PAGE:81==
such workshops, and generally to glean insights into the formation of shared understanding in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
co-design practice. The methodology could also be translated into other domains, such as service
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
design. Moreover, the apparent (though statistically yet to be proven) success of the game offers
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
evidence for the validity of Halstrøm's model of rhetorical design, on which it is based (Halstrøm,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
2017). 
 
​5.1.2​ Answering the research questions 
 
It is time to explicitly answer the first three of my research questions: 
 
1.
What is the theoretical basis of the problem? In other words, what are theoretically the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
bottlenecks that inhibit smooth and fruitful Integrated Design-Build co-design? 
 
The issues in Integrated Design-Build originate from an inadequate alignment of the group
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
members’ mental models. As discussed in Section 2.2, any smooth collaboration requires a shared
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
mental model of the design space, of the processes for conducting the project, and of the team’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
social dynamics. A significant reason for this difficulty can be understood through the lens of
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rhetoric, as shown in Section 2.3: collaborative design is about the justification of decisions by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
persuasive language, which should be molded out by inspection of the design space from various
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
angles. With weak rhetorical capabilities, many people fail to adequately convey their mental
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
models or receive others’. And digging deeper, one finds that this can also be framed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
philosophically as the prevailing of the spirit of individuality that has been pervading especially
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Western culture ever since rationality took root during the Enlightenment, as explained in Section
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
2.1. Collaboration, and more generally social interaction, is understood as an impediment to the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
self’s righteous expression, instead of what it truly is: the ​creation of the self by dwelling richly in
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the world instead of inside one’s own mind (Section 2.1.5).  
 
2.
How can the rhetorical elements that are important in Integrated Design-Build co-design be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
found out? 
 
The first step is gathering data from Integrated Design-Build co-design sessions by recording the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
discussions. This can then be analyzed through the lens of rhetorical topoi, as described in Chapter
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
3. Namely, the recordings should be transcribed and then analyzed either manually or using natural
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
language processing in order to extract commonly occurring topoi (i.e. topics or viewpoints). The
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
resulting collection should then be fine-tuned, dropping topoi that are not conducive of universal
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applicability and comprehension by people of all professional backgrounds (Section 3.1.2). Note that
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
80 
==PAGE:82==
though I employed this process only for Task model topoi, it can just as well be used for the Process
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
and Team models too. 
 
3.
What kind of intervention would be most efficient in achieving the expected outcomes? 
 
A well-designed design game is endowed with a host of values highly relevant to the aims of this
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project: intuitiveness, an engaging quality, idea generation, and so on (Section 2.5). Most notably, it
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
is excellent at disrupting its players’ standard thought processes in a playful fashion, opening their
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
minds to mental model alignment. As for its structure, I deemed it best to base it on Halstrøm’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
 
 
rhetorical-design model (Section 2.3.4) due to the model’s high relevance in enlightening this
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project’s problem space.  
 
 
​5.1.3​ Ways of use 
 
 
Reproducibility 
As the game is supposed to be deployable by any organization, I had its materials’ reproducibility in
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
mind during the design process. All the cards are provided in the Appendices. The metaphor and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topos cards only require printing, cutting and folding, but the value cards (leaves) and the concept
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cards (petals) need holes to be cut. And as it is rather tiresome to craft enough of them for each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
workshop, it would be better to cut them out using a paper cutting machine or laser cutter. The
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
stalks were pieces of rubber-coated metal wire, used for supporting climbing plants, available in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
gardening stores. As for the value bases and the amplification base, they need to be 3D printed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
(unless some energetic facilitator loves wood too much to refrain from wielding the saw and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
drill). Likewise I suggest printing the elements (which I made of wooden balls) that prevent the
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
petals from dropping along the stalks. 
 
 
Sub-initialization 
While I targeted the solution towards the initial stage of Integrated Design-Build projects,
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
particularly the Team Alignment Workshop if such is to be explicitly held, the continuous, nebulous
 
 
 
 
   
     
 
 
 
 
 
 
and sometimes modular nature of Integrated Design-Build may warrant its use later on too. This is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
evident especially in hospital projects. The architecture studio responsible for the hospital case
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
used here, AW2 (which I highly suggest to anyone planning to build sociotechnical healthcare
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
systems), drags its iterative co-design process through several years (at least three), with
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
months-long phases of weekly sessions. Just like the loveliest scrambled eggs are achieved by
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
81 
==PAGE:83==
stirring the eggs for a long time on low heat so that they gradually solidify into a creamy and fluffy
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
sludge, in contrast to quickly frying them into dry pulp (Rense, 2017), AW2 lets the details of each
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project gently coagulate into coherence by a deep interplay of all the stakeholders’ needs and
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expertise through countless working hours and hundreds of nights of conceptual integration in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
deep sleep. The main reason for such a length is the almost unparalleled complexity of the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
requirements for hospitals, with several different departments, each with their own sets of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
expertise to work with, not to speak of their integration into a holistic system. For example, the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
sessions that I observed were about the refinement of floor plans for the maternity and
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
psychological wards, the small groups in each one consisting primarily of nurses and doctors from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the respective ward, alongside one architect. In other words, the hospital’s overall configuration is
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
developed in parallel to each ward.  
There is thus no doubt about the fact that there are Integrated Design-Build cases in which
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
it would be beneficial to deploy my solution multiple times: not only in an initial team alignment
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
session, but also in the first of a series of sessions consisting mostly of new participants (e.g. for
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
each department), and possibly if the focus of design is momentarily shifted to a culturally distinct
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
     
 
 
section of the envisioned system. 
 
 
Breaking free 
Just as Lockton acknowledged for the Design with Intent toolkit (Lockton, 2013), I can now
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
acknowledge that the boundary objects of my solution have high potential to be used in other ways
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
besides the game that I presented. The simplest use of the objects is as generic inspirational
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
material: the topos cards, and to some extent also the metaphor cards, could be used to brainstorm
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
freely in a variety of architectural design situations. Some architects, or design managers in
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
construction companies, could also, if they possess design game knowledge or an ample dose of
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
equivalent intuition, easily craft out another form of game around the materials, or simply tweak
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the rules to better suit their situations. And on the subject of time, it is clear that it is the game’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
     
 
 
most malleable aspect, as I have not found any universally optimal times for each step, and each
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
project is unique in how long a session can be. Each facilitator who is to habitually use this game or
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
a similar one shall simply consult his / her experience of previous sessions to decide how much
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
time to assign. 
The storytelling could be modified in countless ways, for example by creating a scenario in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
pairs vs. as the whole group, and creating them from each person’s ideas vs. from free combination
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
of ideas, and so on. The storytelling could even be expanded into a distinct phase after the Value
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
Finding and the Amplification phases. This ought to be given serious consideration, given the high
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
potential of storytelling, as shown in Section 2.5.5. 
 
 
82 
==PAGE:84==
5.2​ Projection 
 
It is clear that the set of topoi in any possible future version of this game, or another intervention
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that uses a similar analytic process, could be considerably different due to more data. But in what
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
other ways could the methodology be holistically enhanced, perhaps even overhauled? In other
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
words, in this section I answer my fourth research question, “​How can such an intervention be
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
strengthened from a proof of concept into a powerful tool that would be beneficial in any Integrated
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design-Build project?​”  
Below I first explain possible new layerings to the game that could be drawn from the
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
theory that this project sources from. I proceed to proposing automating the topos extraction and
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
strengthening it by a multiplicity of subjectivities. The final discussion is about improving the data
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
collection techniques, making them sounder and better able to cover the object-language
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
continuum.  
 
​5.2.1​ Extra ingredients 
 
There are a number of further aspects of rhetoric and mental models that have some potential for
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
adding value to the game. Firstly, although I translated Halstrøm’s model quite faithfully into the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
realm of design games, I lumped all of my 30 topoi together as ​circumstance topoi​. In other words, I
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
did not incorporate the concept of the ​parallel topoi (​greater​, ​minor​, ​opposite​, ​equal​, ​example and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
comparison​), as explained in Section 2.3.4. (Yet in fact some parallel topoi have managed to sneak into
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
the set, namely ​Dense / sparse​, ​Separate / unified​, and ​Central / peripheral​. You can see this by noting
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that these have no use alone; they have to be applied as adjectives to some ​circumstance topos​,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
whether in the set of 30 or not.) There may well be potential in explicitly adding the parallel topoi
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as an element of the game; they could be used to force the players to mine ever deeper into the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
conceptual veins suggested by each of the 30. One should however keep the time in mind when
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considering such additions, as the average gameplay duration is already fairly long.  
Another ingredient of rhetoric that could be exploited is the ​ethos​, pathos and ​logos​. For
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
example, by making the players use one or all of those three persuasive forms when explaining
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
their ideas, their comprehension of the importance and potential of the idea could possibly be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
enhanced. Note that incorporating this concept effectively into any game would be difficult due to
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
the fact that the interplay of the three forms within collaborative design, and hence any
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
methodology for harnessing them for design practice, is yet to be explored. Moreover, for both this
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
and the above idea of ​parallel topoi​, anyone considering adding such a game element manually via
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
the analytic process that I went through to arrive at the 30 topoi and their mutual connections
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
ought to brace themselves for an arduous trudge, for the ambiguity of the new concepts calls for
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
83 
==PAGE:85==
significant interpretative capacity and reserves of mental energy if they are to be extracted from
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
even a few hours’ worth of transcripts, not to speak of the hundreds of hours that a statistically
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
solid version of such a game (Section 3.3.1) necessitates. To be honest, to preserve your sanity, you
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
should hire a legion of intellectuals to perform such a gargantuan job. 
There is also an important aspect of shared understanding that I omitted when designing
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
the game, namely the concept of the three mental models of collaboration, as elucidated in Section
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
2.2: Task, Process and Team. (The reasons for the omission are listed in Section 3.1.2) Should
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
someone nevertheless want to integrate all three models into a game, there is good news: unlike the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
rhetorical layers above, it is easy to discern which of the three models each utterance in a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
transcript belongs to. They could then be further categorized according to Badke-Schaub’s
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
methodology (e.g. ‘Solution analysis’, ‘Solution evaluation’, and ‘Explanation’ are some of the Task
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
model’s subtypes) (Badke-Schaub & Casakin, 2017). If my analytic technique or the game’s structure
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
is to be used to formulate an intervention for some other design situations than Integrated
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Design-Build sessions (eg. industrial or service design), its different requirements may justify
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
applying the three mental models as another layer of analysis and synthesis. 
 
​5.2.2​ Topos extraction and coding 
 
I outlined a more or less automated way of finding the topoi from transcriptions in Section 3.3.3:
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
using a visual topic model tool, of which Serendip (Alexander et al., 2014) seems to be the most
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
cutting-edge, to cluster words by the LDA algorithm. There I also explained that such a technique
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
does not work with a dataset as small as in this project. But given a repository of hundreds of
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
Integrated Design-Build session recordings, automation would obviously be superior to manual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
topos curation. After transcribing the recordings using an AI tool such as Trint, the result would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
have to be cleaned of the myriad irrelevant linguistic artefacts that always infest recordings of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
natural, volatile speech. And to be able to rip out only the architectural topoi, as I did, utterances
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
belonging to the Process and Team models would have to be omitted.  
The word groups that topic modeling produces would then have to be manually named, i.e.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
each one framed as a topos, for AI currently lacks adequate capabilities of high-level,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
domain-specific synthesis of concepts. Then, just as I did, the resulting numerous topoi should be
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
curated by the (very fuzzy) process that I described in Section 3.1.2. 
Regarding the heuristic nature of my analytic process, described in Section 3.3.1, another
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
researcher to discuss the findings and to check any results with would have alleviated the issue
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
considerably. Particularly the coding would have benefited: having another person independently
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
code at least a subset of the transcripts is a common aspect of this kind of research (e.g.
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Badke-Schaub and Casakin, 2017). And for the topos curation, another person could have come up
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
with more or less topoi, or named them differently; what matters is that by exercising my rhetoric
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
84 
==PAGE:86==
powers with another person, we could have created a shared mental model where much of our
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
individual bias has been filtered off, akin to the way that most genes unique to a certain heritage
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
are left off when that heritage combines with another one, i.e. a ‘mixed-race’ child is born, and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
mostly only the genes that are common throughout humanity are left, yielding children that are
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
healthier and more beautiful than their parents (Ziv, 2016). Another way to understand this benefit
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
is by considering the importance of the ​I-thou relationship (Clair, 2003), expounded in Section 2.1.5.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
Only by a dialectic between the self and others can one truly gain self-consciousness; a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
consciousness of how the self experiences the world, the society, a building; only in the ‘fringes of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
experience’ (James, 1983) can the self be renovated into ever higher levels of empathy and
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
understanding towards any envisioned sociotechnical system and its users and designers, allowing
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
one to discern more accurately the contours of the conceptual space which to delimit into a set of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
architectural topoi. 
 
​5.2.3​ Data collection 
 
I described the accuracy-diminishing and reliability-corroding factors of my data collection
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
technique in Section 3.3.1. Without these, my dataset would have been significantly larger, perhaps
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
over double the current size. The most pernicious of the factors was the recordings’ remarkably low
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
quality, with huge unintelligible swathes, and muddled words littered here and there. This can
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
obviously be solved with better equipment; an external microphone, instead of the standard ones in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
phones and laptops that are designed only for close-up recording.  
Another problem was that all of the groups in the hospital case were in the same space, so
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that it was hard to discern whether a voice belonged to the group under scrutiny or not. The best
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
option in terms of distinguishing voices would be placing a lavalier microphone on each person.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
Note though that even this would not be sufficient if one decides to proceed by the route of AI
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
transcription, for the AI would transcribe the background talk too. The solution would be to feed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
the recordings from all the lavaliers of one group into some algorithm that filters out all the voices
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
whose amplitude does not significantly differ between the microphones. Yet, readers of this work
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
who tend to think outside the box should at this point have come up with a far simpler solution,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
albeit one that cannot always be achieved: asking the session’s facilitators to locate each group in
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
aurally distinct spaces. 
 
85 
==PAGE:87==
 
Figure 25: The language-object continuum. Copied from Fleming, 1998. 
 
In this way the vocal rhetoric that a group emits could be fully captured. But what about the
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rhetoric’s visual facet? Fleming has proposed a continuum (Figure 25) between ​object-laden talk​,
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
where the object leads and language follows, and its counterpart ​language-laden talk (Fleming, 1998).
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Utterances that most strongly constitute the former are categorized as ‘indexing’, which includes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
naming and gesturing; here language is restricted, and dependent on a physical object perceived by
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
all group members. It tends to be sparse and curt. An example, from the hospital case: (Person 1)
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
“​They come from here, so they are… is this a children’s playing area?​” (Person 2) “​Alright, they are there​.​”  
On the other hand, utterances categorized as ‘elaborating’ participate in the invention of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
language-laden talk​, where language is fairly independent of the physical world. Instances include
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
narration and argument; the language is rhetorically rich, and rife with metaphor and opinion. An
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
example, from the hospital case: (talking about low maternity ward intakes) “​I believe the birth rate
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
must increase at some point, otherwise we will go extinct.​”  
The continuum’s midway point, ‘constituting’, is the linguistic core of design. It establishes
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
the object as “​a novel intervention in the physical world, announcing it through assertions of agency, action,
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
and form​” (Fleming, 1998). Such talk is more grounded in the immediate context of design than
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
elaboration, but is more rhetorically constructive than indexing. An example, from the school case:
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
“​The main thing is that we make the connections within the lot, so the supply car would have its own route​.​” 
The language used in the two cases consisted of a rich mixture throughout the continuum.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
Though this can be taken as a sign of a robust design mindset pervading the groups, it was
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
problematic for me due to the large amount of indexing: pointing at the floor plans that lay on
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
tables or hung from walls, enabling the members to easily refer to any space in the envisioned
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
sociotechnical systems. “​The language of design is often highly indexical, so intimately connected to the
 
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
context of its production that it can be removed from that context only by rendering it meaningless​” (Fleming,
   
 
 
   
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
1998). Indeed, we cannot understand what the two people in the first quote above are talking about,
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as we do not see the floor plan.  
In addition to pointing and saying ‘this/that/here’ etc., which is known as ​deixis (“​a form of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
exophoric reference which points to an item "outside" of the speech​”) (Fleming, 1998), indexing includes
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
gesturing, of which the most prominent is hand movement. Kinetic activity that accompanies vocal
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
rhetoric is used to convey and emphasize meaning ; it is “​mate​rial scaffolding that externalizes some
   
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
 
 
 
 
10
10 Not only so, but gesturing may also play an active role in learning by offloading information outside of the speaker’s 
cognition, freeing up cognitive resources for memory. (Goldin​-Meadow and Wagner 2005, p. 237) 
86 
==PAGE:88==
aspects of subjectivity, thus making the mind of another (at least partially) available for perceptual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
engagement​.​” (Krueger, 2013) Bodily expressivity is thinking and feeling in action, as Watsuji (Section
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
2.1.5) framed phenomenologically in his concept of ‘subjective spatiality’. “​Watsuji repeatedly insists
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
that we ought to give explanatory prioritization to the extended human form—and not simply the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
brain—when investigating the basis of social understanding and interpersonal relatedness. The expressive body
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as a whole, in relation to other expressive bodies, is thus the locus of our sociality.​” (Krueger, 2013)  
So what is to be done in future studies to alleviate this weakness? The first step is of course
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
     
 
to record video in addition to audio. This could be somewhat problematic when the primary
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
boundary objects are floor plans printed on paper and laid on tables, like in the hospital case, as the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
camera would have to be suspended above the table. Nevertheless, the video could then be
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
arduously analyzed to extract the meaning of the indexical language peppered throughout (as
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
shown superficially in Fleming, 1998, p. 48). Once again, the enormous quantity of recordings
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
needed to flesh out a robust framework of data presents a problem of time, patience, or both.
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
Automation will be the solution as soon as adequate indexical language recognition AI has been
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
developed. However, for gesturing (i.e. notwithstanding the comparatively trivial ​deixis​), neither
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
manual analysis nor AI would be able to yield maximally useful information without a robust model
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
of its underlying cognitive mechanics. Towards this end, I suggest exploring the perspectives
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
offered by Watsuji’s embodied intersubjectivity, which is backed up by several strands of empirical
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
research (Krueger, 2013). As mentioned in Section 2.1.5, Schroeder and Vallverdú (2015) have already
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
made promising headway with their model of ​situated phenomenology​. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
87 
==PAGE:89==
​6​ Conclusion 
 
 
“​We should now go through our list of objectives; there are multiple reasons for doing so. We
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
clarify to ourselves what we are doing to achieve the client's objectives so that the story gradually
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
takes shape, so that at the end of the development phase we won't have to spend a lot of effort
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
   
   
   
 
searching for arguments for whether we've acted according to the client’s objectives. 
 
We have all kinds of folks coming to this project all the time, and one of [Integrated Design-Build]
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
projects’ problems tends to be that different stakeholders come with their own models of how
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
things should be done. For example, a municipal electrical regulator is used to the task being to
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
 
 
   
catch the contractor of perpetrating as many mistakes as possible [in order to] milk the contractor
 
 
   
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as much as possible​.​” 
The project leader of the school project. 
 
 
“​[The previous phase was holistic but now] we have to dive inside the wards because it may
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
influence the dimensioning, the sizes. We have to constantly envisage what the user experience
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
created by these spaces will be. How pleasant these spaces can be defined. A big unity is handled
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
 
from many angles. To see the innovation possibilities, to make new stuff, and not only what has
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
been done before, but to perceive that we have a different possibility before us.​” 
 
An architect in the hospital project.  
 
 
 
In this project I attempted to design an intervention for the problems that are prevalent in the
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Integrated Design Build co-design process, which are caused by the failure to align expectations of
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
   
the project between all the stakeholders. A related problem that I decided to tackle at the same time
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
   
   
 
 
 
is the occasional failure to consider an Integrated Design-Build project’s design space from all the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
important angles, resulting in the need for creative workarounds later on.   
I approached the task from a theoretical perspective, building up a sound foundation of
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
theoretical knowledge about the design space: the formation of shared mental models, the issues
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
originating from preferring individualism to collectivism, the role of rhetoric in design, and the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
importance of metaphors as a rhetorical element. Next I probed the design space empirically by
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
observing two Integrated Design Build co-design workshops, analyzing the resulting recordings so
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
88 
==PAGE:90==
as to extract a rhetorical base of data, namely a network of general architectural topics. On this
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
base I then built the intervention by the iterative process of alternating development and testing
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
characteristic of action research, coming up with a design game whose structure is equivalent to
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
   
 
 
Per Halstrøm’s rhetorical-design model. The final test that I conducted gave some evidence that the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
game indeed is successful in achieving its first objective, with the alignment of the individual
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
mental models more than doubling. As for the second objective, of design space probing from a
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
wide range of viewpoints, it cannot be said to have been achieved, as the average size of the test
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
group’s mental models did not increase. However, one test is statistically worthless; far more would
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
have to be conducted in order to ascertain the validity of these hypotheses.  
In any case, the promise that the intervention showed during the last test, including the
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
positive qualitative feedback from its participants, suggests that it has the potential to be developed
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
   
 
 
into a highly valuable tool that could indeed eradicate the issues in Integrated Design-Build that
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
originate from poorly shared understanding. I also presented proposals for layering the game with
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
additional theoretical strands and for employing various data collection and analysis techniques.
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Using these, the efficacy of the game (or a similar one built on the same principles) could be raised
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
to such a level that it would be deemed a critical tool in Integrated Design-Build workshops across
 
   
 
   
 
 
   
 
   
 
 
 
 
the world. In addition, the analytical process documented in this thesis could well be found useful
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
as a guide for developing similar rhetorical-design tools for any domain of design.

==PAGE:110==
Appendix D: Topos examples 
 
The following quotes are from the hospital and school cases used in this project. Note that many if not most of 
them contain also other topoi besides the one they have been categorized under. 
 
Central / peripheral 
I think that if we have a finite number of small group spaces, they should all be here in the central 
areas so that they would be findable, so that if we put a separate space here in the wing, no-one 
knows whether it is in use or not, but if they were here near the lobby, everyone could reach them 
and they would act multifunctionally. 
 
Separate / unified 
●
Well, I see it would actually be good to have it here in conjunction with the maternity ward 
as here we have 24/7 operation. 
●
If I could decide, we would have the children’s ward, children’s psychiatry, and children’s 
protection all together. 
●
Person A: ​These rest spaces have been aggregated but maybe they could be divided into 
smaller spaces and again used together. 
Person B:​ It makes monitoring easier, right now we have two so it is hard to monitor. There 
is a possibility for division. 
 
Distance 
Person A:​ No, there is no toilet calculated into them. 
Person B: ​But with children you have almost every day situations where they are in some reception 
and ‘I need to pee’ and it’s five seconds before it’s in their pants, they won’t come running over 
here, so there should be a customer toilet. 
 
Dense / compact 
●
And exactly that the children’s operations, that there will be a dense collaboration, 
children’s neuro is right now on a totally different floor. 
●
This cannot become too dense. The lines of sight have to be preserved. 
 
Logistics 
109 
==PAGE:111==
●
The idea was that the storage of items would be centralized here in the boundary of the 
learning area and the lobby, so that when there is movement between spaces, there is no 
need to fetch it from downstairs, they would be more here in the central area. A challenge is 
that it is not easy to find places to enter, as the supplies take up one connection to the 
lobby. 
●
Person A: ​Not many pupils come anymore from the south, the main mass come from there 
and there. 
Person B:​ All the more reason then to place the building in the north. 
Person A:​ The supply car would come from whichever direction it happens to be coming 
from. 
Person C:​ The main thing is that we make the connections within the lot, so the supply car 
would have its own route. 
 
Congestion 
●
Person A​: Isn’t there a parking place in the current place? 
Person B:​ It’s about there. As I said, it’s been a hellish chaos. I wonder no-one has died 
there. 
●
We will have a challenging situation if our pupils and the shoe storage will come from one 
direction and the evening users from another and we do a double entrance system, so I wish 
we could do it from one entrance, otherwise we waste our precious space." 
 
Future 
(When discussing the low patient intake at the maternity ward) I believe the birth rate must 
increase at some point, otherwise we will go extinct. 
 
Day / night 
Closed, and a view outside, because night nurses watch in the middle of the ward. And it’s probably 
always in use. 
 
Noise 
We have gone through it with zoning, with building control, that there is indeed a line in the 
general zoning plan, the noise area’s boundary. In reality it does not influence the building’s or the 
yard activities’ location in any way. If the building is here, its outer shell will be subject to 3dB 
bigger noise isolation requirements than on this side of the boundary, at least in theory… there is 
no difference in noise in this side and this side of the lot in practice. [But though] it does not affect 
the placement on this lot, got to say that the noise there is hell. When an airplane goes above, you 
cannot hear the person next to you. 
110 
==PAGE:112==
... 
The point was that the building’s inside can be protected from noise, not in the yard, so the most 
logical thing would be to locate the building on the most noisy area. 
 
Folly 
●
Parents are what they are, so if there is a way by which the supply traffic could drive close 
to the building to that side where the intention is not to have cars, however many signs 
there prohibiting it, there will be parents. 
●
The yard is a place where there are a pupils by evening and in weekends. It is fine that they 
are there, but in order for there to be surveilling eyes watching what occurs there, there has 
got to be elements connected to sports and activities so that kids would not always be doing 
foolish stuff together, but there would be other activity stuff due to which, whether adults, 
youngsters or others, would come watch what is happening there. … [Families' coming 
there] has turned out in some cases to be calming. 
 
Sharing 
●
Person A:​ So we were thinking, would it be useful to have various kinds of spaces. A play 
room in the children’s ward, and a living room where parents could watch movies. Like, 
shared spaces. 
Person B:​ Of course the ward’s children are mostly infection children. 
Person A:​ And then all the patient rooms have their own TVs. 
Person C:​ Yea, the day hospital’s guests watch from their own place. 
Person D:​ If there are infection patients, it is indeed probably better to stay in their own 
rooms. 
●
The connection outside from the sports hall is surely important, and what kind of shared 
activities with the city should there be on the school’s lot. What are the primary activities 
on the yard wished by the school, and are there city activities. 
●
It is supposed to be separate. The starting point is that everyone has their workspaces in the 
multispace offices. And there are surely quiet workstations ... how to do the management’s 
workspaces. Do they have separate spaces, or in the multispace offices, so everyone uses the 
same spaces, and we agree on the common archival and storage systems. 
 
Toilet 
●
Person A: ​We have only two toilets here. 
Person B:​ It’s awfully few. 
111 
==PAGE:113==
Person C:​ Actually we’ve been talking that we could make a kind of toilet ‘pool’, and look at 
the whole picture, that there would be toilets where they’re needed, so we would not 
calculate each toilet separately. 
●
These toilet solutions have traditionally been done so that … glass… it could be kept open or 
closed, sometimes they want to see the nurse …  and then the intimacy could be raised by… 
 
Visual connection 
●
We had a nurses’ workspace with glass walls, could see the whole ward. Always saw what 
was happening. 
●
This cannot become too dense. The lines of sight have to be preserved. 
 
Adaptability 
●
(Talking about patient rooms)  
Person A:​ It seems quite a lot to have four families. 
Person B:​ They should be furnitured so that they are adaptable. 
Person C:​ So you could be anywhere. 
●
(Of a proposed patient room) And definitely a partition wall or door, 62 people cannot be on 
the same corridor. 
●
Person A: ​For physical education it would be good if the sports hall remains in physical 
education use, so there would be no chairs, the floor would remain in better condition, and 
if it opens to the lobby it would be really good, but is the space big enough? 
Person B:​ That is the question, and which direction does it open in, and in one or both 
directions, it is one of the functional things that have to be thought of. 
Person C:​ For us, definitely the other direction so the hall remains in physical education 
use. 
●
Person A: ​The seating area means that either the floor is widened, or it is on a balcony, 
above the changing rooms, so it would not be on the hall’s level. 
Person B: ​It is more versatile when it can be opened, pulled in, so other stuff can be done, it 
is only needed in evening. 
 
Extendability 
Person A:​ Is it enlargable so that the end can be extended, or so that there either is or is not a third 
floor. 
Person B: ​Adding a floor is difficult. 
Person A:​ It is pretty difficult, it takes all the spaces underneath away from use. 
Person B: ​In that respect the better option would be an end extendable model, or the hardest, an 
additional wing. As it is, after all, such a small unit in the whole school’s scale. 
112 
==PAGE:114==
Person A: ​It of course affects the configuration of the teaching cells, as smaller units would fit in 
here than if it is this long.
 
113
